Property Six: Essential and Causal Priority Never Becomes Posterior
Razi defines the sixth property of priority and posteriority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between essential/causal priority and temporal priority, and why only temporal priority can reverse.

Core Idea
Essential and causal priority are irreversible; only time-bound priority can flip.

This lesson examines Razi's sixth property concerning priority. The passage distinguishes between two types of priority—essential/causal and temporal—and shows that the former is permanent while the latter can reverse. You'll see how the example of a father and son illustrates this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and its literal translation. Notice the concise style.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two types of priority
The passage mentions two categories: priority by essence/cause, and priority by time. Understand the difference.
3
Analyze the father-son example
Consider how the father is temporally prior to the son, but after the son's death, the father becomes temporally posterior. This illustrates reversal of temporal priority.
Essential priority does not reverse; the father's essential priority over the son (as cause) remains.
4
Grasp the asymmetry for temporal posteriority
The passage notes that something temporally posterior can never become temporally prior to what was originally prior to it. Reflect on why this is obvious.
5
Reflect on philosophical implications
Consider how this property applies to debates about causation and time in kalam. Why is it important?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخاصية السادسة : إن المتقدم بالذات وبالعلية ، لا يصير متأخراً البتة وبالعكس . أما المتقدم بالزمان فإنه قد يصير هو بعينه متأخراً بالزمان ، كالاب فإنه متقدم على الابن بالزمان ، ثم إنه قد يبقى بعد موته ، فيصير متأخراً عنه بالزمان أما المتأخر بالزمان فإنه يمتنع أن يصير متقدماً بالزمان على ما كان متقدماً عليه . وذلك ظاهر.
The sixth property: That which is prior by essence and causation never becomes posterior at all, and vice versa. As for that which is prior by time, it may become exactly posterior by time, like the father: he is prior to the son by time, then he may remain after his death, so he becomes posterior to him by time. As for that which is posterior by time, it is impossible for it to become prior by time to what was prior to it. That is clear.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth property concerning priority and posteriority. It states that essential and causal priority can never turn into posteriority, and vice versa. However, temporal priority can turn into temporal posteriority, as in the example of a father who is temporally prior to his son, but if the son dies first, the father becomes temporally posterior. Conversely, something temporally posterior can never become temporally prior to what was originally prior to it. The conclusion is deemed obvious.

Correction: This property shows that essential and causal priority are fixed; only temporal priority can flip, but only in one direction.

Property Eight: Time as Moving, Space as Still
Razi contrasts time as moving and space as still, declaring time nobler.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that time is nobler than space because motion is superior to stillness.

Core Idea
Motion's nobility makes time superior to space.

The philosophers held a striking view about the nature of time and space. They argued that time is perpetually in motion while space is perfectly still. Since motion is considered more honorable than stillness, they concluded that time is nobler than space. This lesson unpacks that claim, including a citation from a famous sermon.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

Notice the concise style: each clause states a property.
2
Analyze the argument

This is a simple syllogism.
3
Reflect on the citation
The author quotes a philosopher (likely Ibn Sina) stating that space follows time in existence. Consider what 'follows in existence' might mean for their relationship.
This citation reinforces the superiority of time.

The author cites a statement from a 'Shaykh' (likely Ibn Sina or another philosopher) in a famous sermon: 'space follows time in existence and finds it among the first causes of time in determination.'

Property Nine: Space vs. Time in Occupancy
Razi enumerates the ninth property distinguishing space and time.

Learning Objective

Core Idea

In this brief passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents the ninth property distinguishing space and time. He makes a straightforward but profound point: a single spatial unit cannot accommodate two bodies simultaneously, whereas a single temporal unit can host an unlimited number of events. This contrast reveals the unique nature of time as a dimension that permits coexistence.

The Dilemma of Temporal Priority
Razi presents the first question: definition and dilemma of temporal priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ascribing temporal priority leads to a dilemma according to the first question raised by Razi.

Core Idea
Temporal priority's definition collapses upon scrutiny due to a time-based dilemma.

Razi begins a new set of questions for this chapter. The first question challenges the very definition of temporal priority. If something is prior only because it exists earlier, can we even say it is 'prior' when the other does not yet exist? And if both exist later, are they not simultaneous? Let's see how this objection unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of temporal priority
Read the passage's initial definition: something is temporally prior if it exists in a time when the other does not, and later both exist together.
This sets the stage for the objection.
2
Recognize the dilemma: two possible times for ascription
The objector notes that priority could be ascribed either in the first time (when only the prior exists) or in the second time (when both exist).
The passage asks us to consider both options.
3
See why each option fails
First time: priority is relational; without the posterior, no relation. Second time: both exist simultaneously, implying simultaneity, not priority.
Both options are impossible, so the definition leads to contradiction.
4
Reflect on the implication
The objection shows that the common-sense definition of temporal priority may be incoherent, requiring deeper analysis in kalam.
This is a classic problem in philosophical theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول اعلم أنه بقي من مباحث هذا الباب سؤالات : السؤال الأول : إن المتقدم بالزمان هو الذي يكون موجوداً في زمان ، ولا يكون المتأخر عنه موجوداً في ذلك الزمان ، ثم يجيء زمان آخر يحصل فيه كل واحد منهما معاً ، وإذا عرفت هذا فلقائل أن يقول : هذا الشيء المحكوم عليه بالتقدم الزماني إما أن يصير موصوفاً بهذا التقدم الزماني في الزمان الأول ، وهو الزمان الذي كان موجوداً فيه مع عدم هذا المتأخر ، أو يصير موصوفاً بهذا التقدم الزماني في الزمان الثاني وهو الزمان الذي حصل فيه المتقدم والمتأخر معاً . والأول محال ، لأن التقديم من باب المضاف ، فما لم يوجد الغير، لم يكن هو متقدماً عليه . فالزمان الذي لم يحصل فيه التأخر ، امتنع أن يصير الشيء موصوفاً بكونه متقدماً فيه. والثاني أيضاً محال ، لأن الزمان الذي حصل فيه المتأخر يكون ذلك المتقدم حاصلاً مع ذلك المتأخر ، في ذلك الزمان . فعلى هذا التقدير يكون حصولهما في ذلك الزمان موجباً للمعية والاعتبار ،
And we say: Know that there remain from the investigations of this chapter questions: The first question: That which is prior by time is that which exists in a time, and what is posterior to it does not exist in that time, then another time comes in which each of them exists together. And when you understand this, an objector may say: This thing which is judged to have temporal priority either becomes described by this temporal priority in the first time—which is the time in which it existed with the non-existence of this posterior one—or it becomes described by this temporal priority in the second time—which is the time in which both the prior and posterior occur together. The first is impossible, because priority is a relative matter, so as long as the other does not exist, it is not prior to it. So the time in which posteriority does not occur cannot be a time in which the thing is described as being prior. The second is also impossible, because in the time in which the posterior occurs, that prior exists together with that posterior in that time. So on this assumption, their occurrence together in that time necessitates simultaneity and consideration.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage introduces the first question (sual) regarding temporal priority. The author first notes that there remain questions from this chapter. The first question defines temporal priority: something is temporally prior to another if it exists in a time while the other does not, and then there comes a second time when both exist together. An objector then raises a dilemma: either priority is ascribed in the first time (when only the prior exists) or in the second time (when both exist). Both are impossible. In the first time, the posterior does not exist, so priority—being a relative term (min bab al-mudaf)—cannot be attributed because the relation lacks one relatum. In the second time, both exist simultaneously, which implies simultaneity (mu'iyya), not priority. Thus the definition of temporal priority seems to lead to contradiction. The passage ends with 'consideration' (al-i'tibar), suggesting further discussion.

Key Terms

زمان أول
زمان ثان

D) Because time is not real

Key Takeaway
The definition of temporal priority as existence in an earlier time leads to a contradiction because priority requires the other's existence for relation.

The Dilemma of Temporal Priority and Its Resolution
The author presents the first objection on temporal priority and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical problem with attributing temporal priority in external reality and how the author resolves it by locating priority in the mind.

Core Idea
Temporal priority is a mental relation, not an external property.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against the reality of temporal priority and the author's response. The objection shows that if priority is real, it leads to a contradiction; the author resolves it by arguing that priority is a mental consideration, not an external property. You will see how the dilemma unfolds and why the mind is the only place where priority can exist.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of temporal priority
The passage begins by defining the temporally prior: something that exists in a time when the posterior does not, then both exist together later. Grasp this definition before following the objection.
2
Follow the objection's two-option dilemma
The objector asks: does the prior become described as prior in the first time (when only it exists) or in the second time (when both exist)? Both lead to impossibility.
In the first time, the posterior does not exist, so the relation 'prior' cannot hold. In the second time, both exist together, implying simultaneity, which contradicts priority.
3
See why each option fails
Examine the reasoning: priority is a relative term (مضاف) requiring both relata. In the first time, the relatum (posterior) is absent. In the second time, co-existence forces simultaneity, not priority.
4
Grasp the author's answer
The author argues that temporal priority has no existence outside the mind. It arises when the mind considers the father existing before the son; the relation is conceptual.
The example: at the occurrence of the son, the mind recalls the father's earlier existence, creating the relation of priority.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this view resolves the dilemma: since priority is mental, it does not require external simultaneity or a separate time for the relation. It is simply a comparison made by the intellect.
This protects the common-sense understanding of temporal order while avoiding logical contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الأول : إن المتقدم بالزمان هو الذي يكون موجوداً في زمان ، ولا يكون المتأخر عنه موجوداً في ذلك الزمان ، ثم يجيء زمان آخر يحصل فيه كل واحد منهما معاً ، وإذا عرفت هذا فلقائل أن يقول : هذا الشيء المحكوم عليه بالتقدم الزماني إما أن يصير موصوفاً بهذا التقدم الزماني في الزمان الأول ، وهو الزمان الذي كان موجوداً فيه مع عدم هذا المتأخر ، أو يصير موصوفاً بهذا التقدم الزماني في الزمان الثاني وهو الزمان الذي حصل فيه المتقدم والمتأخر معاً . والأول محال ، لأن التقديم من باب المضاف ، فما لم يوجد الغير، لم يكن هو متقدماً عليه . فالزمان الذي لم يحصل فيه التأخر ، امتنع أن يصير الشيء موصوفاً بكونه متقدماً فيه. والثاني أيضاً محال ، لأن الزمان الذي حصل فيه المتأخر يكون ذلك المتقدم حاصلاً مع ذلك المتأخر ، في ذلك الزمان . فعلى هذا التقدير يكون حصولهما في ذلك الزمان موجباً للمعية والاعتبار ، والذي يكون موجباً للمعية يمتنع أن يكون هو بعينه موجباً للتقدم ، لأن المعية منافية للتقدم . فيثبت : أنه لم يحصل لهذا المتقدم إلا هذا الزمان ، ويثبت : أن كل واحد منهما ينافي حصول التقدم، فوجب أن لا يصح الحكم على الشيء بكونه متقدماً على الغير بالزمان . فهذا هو تقرير هذا الشك. وجوابه : أن التقدم بالزمان لا حصول له إلا في الأذهان . وذلك لأن عند حصول الابن يعتبر الذهن أن الأب كان موجوداً قبله . فمن هذا الاعتبار يحصل التقدم الزماني
The first question: The temporally prior is that which exists in a time, and the posterior does not exist in that time, then another time comes in which both occur together. And if you understand this, one may say: This thing judged as temporally prior either becomes described with this temporal priority in the first time, which is the time in which it existed while this posterior was absent, or becomes described with this temporal priority in the second time, which is the time in which both the prior and posterior occurred together. The first is impossible because priority is a relative thing; as long as the other does not exist, it is not prior to it. So in the time in which the posterior did not occur, it is impossible for the thing to be described as being prior in it. The second is also impossible because in the time in which the posterior occurred, the prior occurs together with that posterior in that time. So according to this estimation, their occurrence in that time necessitates simultaneity and consideration, and that which necessitates simultaneity is impossible to be exactly what necessitates priority, because simultaneity is contrary to priority. Thus it is established that this prior has no time except this [latter] time, and it is established that each of the two times precludes the occurrence of priority, so it must be that judgment of a thing being temporally prior to another is not valid. This is the formulation of this doubt. And its answer: Temporal priority has no existence except in the minds. That is because at the occurrence of the son, the mind considers that the father existed before him. From this consideration, temporal priority occurs.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection concerning temporal priority and the author's answer. The objection argues that it is impossible for something to be truly temporally prior to another. The argument considers two possible times: the first time when only the prior exists and the second when both exist. In the first, priority cannot be attributed because the posterior does not exist to serve as the relation. In the second, priority cannot be attributed because the two exist together, implying simultaneity, which contradicts priority. Therefore, temporal priority cannot be externally realized. The author responds that temporal priority is not an external reality but a mental consideration: when the son appears, the mind recalls that the father existed earlier, and thus the relation of priority is conceptual.

Key Terms

المعية

الذهن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporal priority is an objective feature of the external world, existing independently of the mind.
Correction: The author argues that temporal priority has no existence except in the minds (الذهان), as illustrated by the father-son example.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer, where does temporal priority exist?
A) In external reality
B) Only in the mind
C) In the first time
D) In the second time

Key Takeaway
Temporal priority is not an external attribute but a mental relation that arises when the mind compares earlier and later existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author concludes that temporal priority is a mental consideration, not an external reality. When you reflect on your own past sins or good deeds, are you tempted to see them as fixed 'prior' events that absolutely define you? How would viewing their priority as a mental construct affect your sense of repentance and hope in Allah's mercy? What small habit can you adopt today to remind yourself that Allah's forgiveness transcends time?
?
Consider the father-son example: the son's existence triggers the mental recognition of the father's priority. In your life, what current blessings remind you of Allah's prior grace? How often do you consciously thank Allah for blessings that you only now recognize as having been preceded by His mercy? What will you do tomorrow to turn a common experience into a moment of realizing Allah's priority?

Resolving the Objection on Priority and Posteriority as Relations
Second objection on priority and posteriority as simultaneous relations and its resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second objection to priority and posteriority as simultaneous relations is resolved by distinguishing between essence and relational consideration.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority are simultaneous as relations but not as essences.

This passage presents a subtle objection: if priority and posteriority are relational terms, they must be simultaneous, but priority implies non-simultaneity. The resolution lies in distinguishing between the actual prior essence and the mental relation. Let's see how the author untangles this apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the argument that priority and posteriority as relations are simultaneous, leading to a contradiction.
Focus on the logical steps: from 'priority and posteriority are relatives' to 'they are simultaneous' to 'priority contradicts simultaneity'.
2
Identify the key terms
Note the Arabic terms: مضافان (relatives), تقدم (priority), تأخر (posteriority), مقارنة (concomitance), اعتبار (consideration).
These terms are central to the argument and its resolution.
3
Analyze the answer
See how the author distinguishes between the essence (ذات) of the father being prior and the relational description (كونه متقدماً) being concomitant with the son's posteriority.
The distinction in consideration (تغاير في الاعتبار) removes the contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : إن التقدم والتأخر مضافان ، والمضافان معاً . فالتقدم مع التأخر والمعية ينافي التقدم. والحاصل أن كونه متقدماً على الغير ، يوجب كونه ذلك الغير ، وكونه معه ينافي كونه متقدماً عليه . ينتج أن كونه متقدماً على الغير ، ينافي كونه متقدماً على الغير ، وذلك باطل . والجواب : إن ذات الأب متقدمة على ذات الابن . أما كونه متقدماً على الابن فإنه مقارن لكون الابن متأخراً عنه. ولما حصل التغاير في الاعتبار ، زال التنافي .
The second question: Priority and posteriority are two relatives, and two relatives are simultaneous. So priority with posteriority and simultaneity contradicts priority. The upshot is that being prior to another necessitates being that other, and being with him contradicts being prior to him. It follows that being prior to another contradicts being prior to another, and that is false. And the answer: The essence of the father is prior to the essence of the son. As for his being prior to the son, it is concomitant with the son's being posterior to him. And when the distinction in consideration is realized, the contradiction ceases.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage contains the second objection and its answer. The objection points out that priority and posteriority are relational terms (مضافان) and thus should be simultaneous. But priority implies non-simultaneity, leading to a contradiction: being prior requires the other to exist as prior's opposite, but simultaneity negates priority. The author resolves this by distinguishing between the essence (ذات) and the relational description: the essence of the father is prior to the essence of the son in reality, but the description of being prior is simultaneous with the description of being posterior in the mind. Since the consideration is different, there is no contradiction.

Key Terms

التأخر
المضافان
المقارنة
الاعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objection disproves causal priority entirely.
Correction: The answer shows the objection only applies to the relational description, not to the essential priority of the father over the son.

Checkpoint

A) By denying relational terms
B) By distinguishing essence from relational consideration
C) By asserting both are simultaneous
D) By rejecting the objection

Key Takeaway
The contradiction in priority and posteriority as simultaneous relations is resolved by distinguishing the essence (which is actually prior) from the relational description (which is concomitant).

The Third Objection: Attributes of Non-Existent and Temporal Change
Objection against attributes of non-existent and change of temporal descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical problem raised by the third objection: how describing a non-existent as 'will exist' or 'was past' implies an attribute subsisting in a non-existent subject, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Temporal attributes like 'future' and 'past' cannot subsist in a non-existent being.

The third objection in the text targets the idea that a non-existent thing can have real attributes of futurity or pastness. The objector argues that such descriptions would require an attribute to exist before its subject, which is rationally impossible. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step, even though the answer is not provided here.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it has two main parts: one about future existence before coming into being, and another about past existence after perishing.
The answer is not included in this passage, so focus on understanding the problem itself.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The objection presents two cases: (1) describing a non-existent as 'will exist', and (2) after existence and perishing, describing it as 'past'. Both claim that the description implies an attribute that exists while the subject does not.
3
Trace the logical impossibility
Follow the argument: if a description occurs before the subject exists, then that description (as an existent thing) must subsist in a non-existent subject, which is impossible. Similarly, the description 'past' is different from the essence, as shown by shared pastness among different essences.
This challenges the reality of temporal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : المعدوم قبل دخوله في الوجود يصدق عليه أنه سيوجد ، ثم إذا صار موجوداً ، فإنه يزول عنه وصف أنه سيوجد ، فهذا وصف قد عدم بعد حصوله ، فيكون وصفاً موجوداً . لكنه إنما حصل قبل دخوله في الوجود ، فيلزم قيام الصفة الموجودة بالموصوف المعدوم ، وأنه محال . وأيضاً : أنه إذا وجد وحضر ، ثم فني وعدم بعد ذلك ، فإنه يحصل له وصف كونه ماضياً . وهذا الوصف مغاير لتلك الماهية المخصوصة. بدليل : أن الماهيات المختلفة قد تكون منشاركة في صيرورتها ماضية ، والشيء الواحد يتوارد عليه وصف كونه مستقبلاً وحاضراً وماضياً . وكل ذلك يدل على أن وصف كونه ماضياً مغاير لتلك الذات
The third question: The non-existent before entering into existence is truly described as that it will exist. Then when it becomes existent, the description that it will exist ceases from it. So this description has ceased after its occurrence, so it is an existent description. But it occurred before entering into existence, so it necessitates the subsisting of an existent attribute in a non-existent subject, and that is impossible. Also, if it exists and is present, then perishes and becomes non-existent after that, it acquires the description of being past. And this description is different from that particular essence. Evidence: different essences may share in becoming past, and one thing can bear the descriptions of being future, present, and past successively. All of that indicates that the description of being past is different from that essence.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection without its answer. The objection focuses on the attributes of future existence, present existence, and past existence. The objector argues that describing a non-existent as 'will exist' implies an attribute (future existence) that exists before the thing exists, which would require an attribute to subsist in a non-existent subject, an impossibility. Similarly, after the thing perishes, it acquires the attribute of being past, which is different from the essence. The objection aims to show that these temporal attributes cannot be real because they would require an impossible relation. Since the answer is not included in this window, the discussion is incomplete.

Correction: The objection is not denying that things have temporal descriptions; it is arguing that if these descriptions were real attributes, they would require an impossible subsisting relation.

Objection and Answer: Are Past and Future Real Attributes?
Objection and two answers on attributing existence to past/future entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical problem of predicting past and future attributes to non-existent entities and the preferred theological solution that such attributes are not real existent qualities.

Core Idea
Past and future are not real attributes—they are mental considerations.

Theologians face a puzzle: when we say a thing 'was existent' or 'will exist', that description seems to be a real attribute occurring in the present. Yet it describes something that no longer exists or does not yet exist. How can an existent attribute subsist in a non-existent? This passage presents the objection, two attempted answers, and resolves the matter with the view that being past and future are not actually existent attributes at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the opening lines of the Arabic passage, which state that a description like 'will exist' is an existent attribute but applies only after the thing becomes non-existent, leading to an impossibility.
Notice the logical structure: a premise, a consequence, and a conclusion that it is impossible.
2
Identify the first answer
The first answer claims that the subject of such judgments is the mental image (al-ṣūra al-dhihniyya), which is present and existent in the mind. This avoids the problem of an attribute subsisting in a pure non-existent.
This answer is attributed to 'some people' (min al-nās).
3
Understand the counter-objection
The objector replies that the mental image is present, and what is present cannot be past or future. So this answer does not truly resolve the issue.
The objection highlights that presence contradicts past/future.
4
Follow the dilemma and the conclusion
The author sums up: the thing judged as past or future is either present (then not past/future) or not present (then non-existent, but the attribute is existent, again impossible). The preferred solution is that being past and future are not two existent attributes at all.
This final view ('al-aqrab') avoids the whole problem by denying that these are real attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المخصوصة. وهذا الوصف لا شك أنه وصف موجود بدليل أنه حصل بعد أن لم يكن حاصلاً، لكن هذا الوصف إنما يحصل للشيء بعد صيرورته معدوماً، فيلزم قيام الصفة الموجودة بالمعدوم المحض، وهو محال. والجواب: من الناس من قال: المحكوم عليه بأنه سيوجد، أو بأنه كان موجوداً: هو الصورة الذهنية الحاضرة في العقل. وهي موجودة لا معدومة. ولقائل أن يقول: الصورة الذهنية حاضرة، والحاضر من حيث إنه حاضر لا يكون ماضياً ولا مستقبلاً. والحاصل: أن المحكوم عليه بأنه ماضي وبأنه مستقبل، إما أن يكون موجوداً حاضراً أو لا يكون. فإن كان حاضراً لم يكن ماضياً ولا مستقبلاً، وإن لم يكن حاضراً كان معدوماً في الحال. وصف كونه ماضياً ومستقبلاً موجود في الحال. فيلزم قيام الصفة الموجودة بالمعدوم المحض والنفي الصرف. وهو محال. والأقرب أن يقال: كونه ماضياً ومستقبلاً ليسا صفتين موجودتين والله أعلم.
The specific [description]. And this description is undoubtedly an existent description, by evidence that it occurs after not having occurred. But this description only obtains for the thing after it becomes non-existent. So it necessitates the subsistence of the existent attribute in the pure non-existent, which is impossible. The answer: Among people there is one who says: The thing judged that it will exist, or that it was existent, is the mental image present in the mind. And it is existent, not non-existent. And one may object: The mental image is present, and the present insofar as it is present is neither past nor future. The conclusion: The thing judged as past and as future either is an existent present thing or not. If it is present, it is not past nor future. If it is not present, it is non-existent at present. And the description of being past and future is existent at present. So it necessitates the subsistence of the existent attribute in the pure non-existent and absolute negation, which is impossible. And the closest [view] is to say: Its being past and future are not two existent attributes. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument concerning the logical problem of predicating past and future attributes to things. The objection begins by stating that a description (e.g., 'will exist' or 'was existent') is undoubtedly an existent attribute, since it occurs after not having occurred. However, this description applies to a thing only after it becomes non-existent (i.e., the thing is past or future, not present). This leads to the impossibility of an existent attribute subsisting in a pure non-existent. The author then presents two answers. The first answer, attributed to some scholars, holds that the subject of judgment (the thing said to be past or future) is the mental image (الصورة الذهنية) present in the mind, which is existent, not non-existent. But an objector replies that the mental image is present, and the present qua present is neither past nor future. The author then sums up the dilemma: the thing judged as past or future either is present (in which case it cannot be past or future) or is not present (in which case it is non-existent now, yet the attribute 'past' or 'future' is existent now, leading again to the impossible subsistence of an attribute in a non-existent). The author concludes that the closest correct view is that being past and future are not two existent attributes at all—they are relational or non-entitative descriptions.

Key Terms
الصفة
المعدوم المحض
الصورة الذهنية
الحاضر
الماضي
المستقبل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that past and future are real attributes inhering in objects, like color or size.
Correction: The passage argues that being past and future are not existent attributes; they are merely relational or mental considerations, not real qualities.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The Third Argument: Linguistic Impossibility of Non-Existence
A linguistic proof against imagining non-existence, with a brief reference to another group's proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the words 'was' and 'will be' necessarily imply time periods, and how this demonstrates the impossibility of conceiving the non-existence of an eternal being.

Core Idea
The language of 'was' and 'will be' shows that non-existence requires time.

This passage presents a subtle linguistic argument that the act of imagining non-existence presupposes a temporal framework. By analyzing the necessary implications of the words we use, the author shows that the intellect itself cannot conceive of the non-existence of the eternal. This argument complements rational proofs by grounding them in the very structure of language and thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style; the argument is packed into few lines.
2
Identify the main claim
The author claims that saying something 'was' or 'will be' non-existent necessarily refers to a past or future period. This implies that non-existence is temporal.
This is the core linguistic premise.
3
Examine the objection and its refutation
Some might object that this is just a limitation of language. The author refutes this by stating that it is because the intellect cannot assume non-existence at all.
The refutation shifts from language to rational capacity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that assuming non-existence of the eternal is rejected by plain intellect and understanding. This shows the impossibility of conceiving non-existence.
This aligns with the broader theme of the lesson.
5
Note the reference to other proofs
The author mentions another group who proved the eternity of time but declines to repeat their arguments. This does not affect the main argument.
This reference is for completeness; focus on the main argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنا متى قلنا : إنه كان معدوماً أو سيصير معدوماً . فلفظ كان ، وسيكون ، لا بد وأن يشير إلى مدة ماضية ، وإلى مدة لاحقة آتية . وقول من يقول : إن هذا لضيق العبارة كلام فاسد، بل هذا لأجل أن العقل لا يتمكن من فرض عدمه ، والفهم لا يمكنه التعبير عن تقدير عدمه . وكل ذلك لاجل ما ادعينا أن تقدير عدمه وفرض رفعه ، كما يأباه صريح العقل والفهم . وأما الطائفة الأخرى : وهم الذين يثبتون كون المدة قديمة بالحجة والبينة ، فقد ذكرنا في كتاب القدم والحدوث : الوجوه التي ذكروها وهولوا عليها : فلا فائدة في الإعادة
Third: Whenever we say: 'It was non-existent' or 'it will become non-existent,' the words 'was' and 'will be' must refer to a past period and to a subsequent future period. The saying of one who says that this is due to the restriction of expression is corrupt speech; rather, this is because the intellect cannot assume its non-existence, and the understanding cannot express the estimation of its non-existence. And all of that is because of what we claimed: that the estimation of its non-existence and the assumption of its removal is rejected by plain intellect and understanding. As for the other group—those who affirm that time is eternal with proof and evidence—we have mentioned in the Book of Eternity and Temporality the aspects they mentioned and exaggerated; so there is no benefit in repetition.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is the third argument in a series (the first two are not included here) addressing the impossibility of conceiving the non-existence of an eternal being. The author argues that the very words 'was' and 'will be' necessarily imply a past and future period; thus one cannot legitimately say that something was or will be non-existent without referring to a time. To claim this is a mere verbal limitation is false—rather, it reflects the fact that reason is incapable of assuming non-existence, and understanding cannot express it. The author then mentions another group who prove the eternity of time with evidence, referring to a previous work, and declines to repeat those proofs. The argument establishes a linguistic and rational necessity that eternal existence cannot be imagined as non-existent.

Key Terms
مدة
قديم
عدم
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is merely making a linguistic point without rational weight.
Correction: The argument is a rational proof grounded in the necessary implications of language, showing that the intellect cannot conceive of non-existence without time.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The linguistic necessity of time in expressions of non-existence proves that the eternal being cannot be conceived as non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Defining al-Midda (Duration) and Its Perceptual Basis
Definition of al-midda and explanation of its perceptual basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-midda (duration) as a continuous existent and how the mind perceives it through the succession of present moments.

Core Idea
Duration is a real, continuous existent perceived through the succession of 'now' moments.

This lesson introduces the first of several key terms: al-midda (duration). The author begins by noting the subtlety of these concepts. He then defines duration as an inherently continuous entity, explaining that our awareness of it arises from the ongoing flow of present moments. This foundation is crucial for understanding subsequent discussions on time and change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Notice the author's emphasis on subtlety.
2
Identify the definition of duration
Find the phrase where the author describes what duration is in its essence: 'موجود باقي دائم مستمر' (existent, remaining, perpetual, continuous).
3
Understand the perceptual mechanism
See how the author explains that the mind perceives duration through the succession (تعاقب) and alternation (توالي) of present moments.
This is key: duration is real but perceived via succession.
4
Connect to the name 'midda'
Note the reasoning: the name 'midda' (duration) comes from the impression that each moment extends the previous one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم . أن إيضاح الفرق بين مفهومات هذه الألفاظ دقيقة غامضة في المعقولات . فاللفظ الأول : مدة . مبني على مباحث المدة : وقد ذكرنا أنها في ذاتها وفي جوهرها موجود باقي دائم مستمر ، إلا أن شعور العقل بها إنما يحصل بسبب توالي الآنات الحاضرة ، وتعاقبها . فهذا التعاقب والتوالي يشبه كأن بعضها مدد للبعض في الوجود ، أو يقال كان الزمان يمتد بسبب تعاقبها وتواليها . فلهذا السبب سميت بالمدة
Know that clarifying the difference between the concepts of these terms is a subtle and obscure matter in rational sciences. The first term: duration (al-midda). It is based on investigations of duration: we have stated that it is in its essence and its substance existent, remaining, perpetual, continuous, but the mind's perception of it only occurs due to the succession and alternation of present moments. This alternation and succession resembles as if some of them are extensions for others in existence, or it is said that time extends due to their succession and alternation. For this reason it was called duration.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage defines the first term, al-midda (duration). The author begins by noting that distinguishing these concepts is subtle and obscure. He then explains that al-midda is a self-subsistent, eternal, continuous entity. However, our awareness of it arises only from the ongoing succession of 'now' moments. This succession gives the impression that each moment extends the previous one, hence the name 'duration' (midda). The definition emphasizes that duration is real and objective, not merely subjective, but its perception depends on temporal succession.

Definition of Time and Its Independence from Motion
The author defines time and argues its independence from motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that time exists independently of motion, and motion only serves to measure it.

Core Idea
Time is real and self-sufficient; motion only measures it.

In this passage, the author introduces the concept of time as an independent entity. He argues that motion does not create time but merely provides a means to measure it. This distinction is crucial for later theological discussions about the nature of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Let the author's claim sink in.
The passage begins with a reminder of a prior proof.
2
Analyze the sundial analogy
The sundial does not create the day but divides it. Similarly, motion does not create time but measures it. Notice how the analogy clarifies the relationship.
3
Understand why celestial motion is chosen
Celestial motion is the fastest and most regular, making it the best standard for measurement. Reflect on why this matters for theological arguments.
This connects to the concept of estimation (taqdīr).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللفظ الثاني : الزمان : واعلم أنا بينا أن الزمان موجود غني في ذاته وفي وجوده عن الحركة ، بل هو حاصل ، سواء حصلت الحركة أم لا ، على هذا التقدير نقول : إنه لا تأثير للحركة في وجود المدة والزمان. إنما تأثير الحركة في تقديرها وتحديدها، كما أن البنكانات وسائر الآلات ، قد تقدر مدة اليوم بالأجزاء والأبعاض ، ثم إن البنكان لا تأثير له في تكوين اليوم وإيجاده ، وإنما تأثيره في تقدير أجزائه وأبعاضه . فكذلك ههنا حركة الفلك لا تأثير لها في إيجاد المدة وإنما تأثيرها في تقدير المدة ، وإنما تقدرت المدة بالحركة الفلكية لا بسائر الحركات ، لأنها أسرع الحركات وأبعدها عن الاختلافات، فلا جرم جعلت هذه الحركة مقدرة للمدة .
The second term: time (al-zaman). Know that we have clarified that time is existent, independent in its essence and existence from motion; rather, it is present regardless of whether motion occurs or not. On this basis we say: motion has no effect on the existence of duration and time. Motion only affects their estimation and measurement, just as sundials and other instruments may estimate the duration of a day into parts and segments; the sundial has no effect on creating the day, only on estimating its parts and segments. Similarly, here, the motion of the sphere has no effect on causing duration, only on estimating it. Duration is estimated by celestial motion, not by other motions, because it is the fastest motion and farthest from irregularities; hence this motion is made the measure of duration.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage defines al-zaman (time). The author asserts that time is a real, self-sufficient entity, independent of motion. Motion does not bring time into existence; it only provides a measure for it. The analogy of a sundial is used: the sundial does not create the day but divides it. Similarly, celestial motion (the fastest and most regular) is used to measure duration, not to create it. This distinction is crucial for later arguments about time being created or eternal.

First Branch: Mutual Measurement of Time and Motion
The author explains mutual estimation of time and motion with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how time and motion can measure each other from different perspectives based on the container-content analogy.

Core Idea
Time and motion measure each other, but from opposite directions.

This passage explores a key relation in the philosophy of time and motion: each can serve as a measure for the other, but only from distinct standpoints. The author uses a simple analogy of a container and its contents to clarify this reciprocal estimation, then applies it to concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's flow come through first.
Notice the structure: general statement, analogy, then two examples.
2
Understand the container-content analogy
The author compares time to a container and motion to its contents. Just as knowing the capacity tells you the amount of contents, and vice versa, time and motion can measure each other depending on what is known.
The analogy is key to grasping the reciprocal measurement.
3
Apply the analogy to the examples
Consider the two examples: a bowshot (known motion) measures unknown time, and an hour's journey (known time) measures unknown motion. See how each serves as a measure from a different aspect.
These examples illustrate the two different perspectives mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : يتفرع على ما ذكرناه فرعان : الأول : إن الزمان والحركة يقدر كل واحد منهما بالآخر ، لكن لا من وجه آخر بل بحسب وجهين (۱) مختلفين : وذلك لأن الزمان ظرف ، والحركة مظروف، ويجوز تقدر كل واحد منهما بالآخر ، فتارة يقال : هذا المكيل خمسة أمناء ، وذلك إذا عرفت أولاً : أن ذلك الكيل لا يحتمل إلا خمسة أمناء ، وأخرى يقال : هذا الكيل لا يحتمل إلا خمسة أمناء ، إذا عرفت أن هذا المكيل خمسة أمناء ، فكذا ههنا . تارة يقال : هذا الزمان زمان غلوة ، وذلك إذا كان قدر الزمان مجهولاً وكان قدر الحركة معلوماً وتارة يقال مسيرة ساعة ، وذلك إذا كان الزمان معلوماً ، وكان قدر الحركة مجهولاً (١) .
If you know this, we say: two branches derive from what we mentioned. The first: time and motion can each be measured by the other, but not from a single aspect, rather according to two different aspects. That is because time is a container and motion is its content, and it is permissible for each to measure the other. Sometimes it is said: this measure is five pounds, if you first know that that measure holds only five pounds. Another time it is said: this measure holds only five pounds, if you know that this measured thing is five pounds. Similarly here: sometimes it is said: this time is the time of a bowshot, when the quantity of time is unknown and the quantity of motion is known. Sometimes it is said: an hour's journey, when time is known and the quantity of motion is unknown.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents the first branch of consequences. It establishes a reciprocal relationship between time and motion: each can measure the other from different perspectives. The author uses an analogy of a container and its contents: if you know the capacity, you can measure the contents, and vice versa. Similarly, if motion is known (e.g., a bowshot), it measures unknown time; if time is known (e.g., an hour), it measures unknown motion. This highlights the interdependence in estimation without implying ontological dependence.

Correction: The author clarifies that the mutual measurement is only in terms of estimation, not ontological dependence. Each can be known independently, but when one is unknown, the other serves as a measure.

Checkpoint

Distinguishing Eternal Duration from Created Time
The author resolves a paradox by distinguishing eternal midda from created zaman measured by motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the apparent contradiction between the eternity of duration and the createdness of time by distinguishing the underlying substance from its measurement by motion.

Core Idea
Duration is the eternal substance; time is that substance measured by motion.

Ancient scholars stated that duration (midda) has no beginning and that time (zaman) is originated. This seems contradictory. In this passage, the author resolves the paradox by clarifying that 'duration' refers to the eternal substance itself, while 'time' refers to that same substance when considered as measured by motion. Since motion is created, the measurement is created, so time indeed has a beginning. This semantic distinction preserves both truths.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the paradox and the author's resolution.
Note the footnote markers; they indicate references in the original text.
2
Identify the two definitions
Notice how the author defines 'midda' as the substance itself, and 'zaman' as the duration measured by motion.
3
Trace the logical resolution
Understand why the paradox dissolves: since motion is created, the measurement (time) is also created, while the underlying substance (duration) remains eternal.
This shows how a semantic distinction can resolve a theological puzzle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثاني : من الناس من جعل المدة اسماً لجوهر هذا الموجود ، وأما الزمان فإنه جعله اسماً للمدة المتقدرة بالحركة ، والذي حمله على هذا القول ما ورد في كلام المتقدمين أن المدة والدهر لا أول له ، ثم قالوا : الزمان محدث فلها أراد هذا القائل إزالة التناقض قال المدة والدهر (۳) اسمان لذات هذا الشيء (٤) وجوهره ، وهي قديمة. وأما الزمان فهو اسم للمدة حال كونها متقدرة بالحركة ، ولما كانت الحركة حادثة كان كون المدة متقدرة بها حادثاً ، فحينئذ صح قولهم : الزمان محدث وله أول .
The second branch: among people there are those who make 'duration' (al-midda) a name for the substance of this existent, but 'time' (al-zaman) they make a name for the duration measured by motion. What led them to this statement is what appeared in the speech of the ancients that duration and eternity have no beginning, then they said: time is originated. So when this speaker wanted to remove the contradiction, he said: duration and eternity are two names for the essence of this thing and its substance, and it is eternal. As for time, it is a name for duration at the time of being measured by motion. Since motion is originated, the fact that duration is measured by it is originated. Then their statement is correct: time is originated and has a beginning.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage resolves an apparent contradiction in earlier claims: the ancients said duration (midda) is eternal, but time (zaman) is created. The solution is to treat midda as the eternal substance itself, while zaman is that same substance when considered as measured by motion. Since motion is created (hadith), the measurement is also created, so time has a beginning. This semantic distinction preserves both the eternity of the underlying reality and the createdness of time as we experience it.

Definition of al-Waqt (Moment) as a Distinct Part
Definition of al-waqt (moment) as a discrete part distinguished by an event.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
A moment is not just any time unit—it is defined by a known event.

In kalam, time is not just an abstract continuum. The term 'al-waqt' (moment) refers to a specific discrete part of time that is recognized because of a particular event that occurs within it. This lesson unpacks that definition and provides an example.

Guided Path

The definition is concise; each phrase adds an essential condition.
2

The event must be known in advance to define the moment.
3
Connect to common misunderstanding

The answer is no—without an event, there is no distinction.

Distinction Between Duration and Time
The author defines المدة as the essence of the existent and الزمان as duration measured by motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between المدة (duration) and الزمان (time) as proposed by some scholars to resolve the apparent contradiction that المدة is eternal while الزمان is originated.

Core Idea
المدة is the eternal essence; الزمان is the same duration measured by motion, thus originated.

In this passage, the author presents a nuanced theological distinction that resolves an apparent contradiction in the statements of the ancients. By separating the eternal essence of a thing from its temporal measurement, a coherent understanding of time emerges. Let's explore how this distinction works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms المدة, الزمان, الدهر, جوهر, محدث.
The author is reporting a view, not stating his own position.
2
Identify the contradiction
The ancients said al-midda and al-dahr have no beginning, but also that al-zaman is originated. This seems contradictory. Find where this is stated.
3
Understand the resolution
The proposed view says al-midda is the eternal substance, while al-zaman is that same duration when measured by motion, which is originated. See how this resolves the contradiction.
Motion is originated, so the measurement is originated.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for theology. It allows for an eternal underlying reality while maintaining that time as we experience it has a beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثاني : من الناس من جعل المدة اسماً لجوهر هذا الموجود ، وأما الزمان فإنه جعله اسماً للمدة المتقدرة بالحركة ، والذي حمله على هذا القول ما ورد في كلام المتقدمين أن المدة والدهر لا أول له ، ثم قالوا : الزمان محدث فلما أراد هذا القائل إزالة التناقض قال المدة والدهر اسمان لذات هذا الشيء وجوهره ، وهي قديمة. وأما الزمان فهو اسم للمدة حال كونها متقدرة بالحركة ، ولما كانت الحركة حادثة كان كون المدة متقدرة بها حادثاً ، فحينئذ صح قولهم : الزمان محدث وله أول .
The second branch: Some people made al-midda (duration) a name for the essence (jawhar) of this existent, and as for al-zaman (time), they made it a name for the duration measured by motion. What led them to this view is what was mentioned in the speech of the ancients that al-midda and al-dahr (perpetuity) have no beginning, then they said: al-zaman is originated (muḥdath). Then their statement becomes correct: al-zaman is originated and has a beginning.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents a definition and argument from the second branch. The author reports a view that distinguishes between al-midda (duration) and al-zaman (time). Some scholars made al-midda refer to the essence (jawhar) of the existing thing, and al-zaman refer to that same duration but as measured by motion. The reason for this distinction is to reconcile statements from the ancients: they said that al-midda and al-dahr (perpetuity) have no beginning, but also that al-zaman is originated (ḥādith). To remove the apparent contradiction, they said: al-midda and al-dahr are names for the eternal essence of this thing, while al-zaman is the duration considered with respect to being measured by motion. Since motion is originated, the duration's being measured by motion is also originated, and thus al-zaman is originated and has a beginning. This resolves the contradiction.

The Meaning of al-Waqt (Time/Instant)
Definition of al-waqt as a specific part of duration marked by a known event.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of al-waqt (time/instant) and how it differs from related terms like al-midda and al-zaman.

Core Idea
Al-waqt is a specific part of duration identified by a known event.

In Islamic theology, precise terminology is crucial. The term 'al-waqt' is often used interchangeably with 'time' in everyday language, but scholars distinguish it carefully from other temporal concepts. This passage from al-Matalib al-'Aliya clarifies what al-waqt really means by contrasting it with al-midda (duration) and al-zaman (time as measured by motion).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the definition of al-waqt and how it is distinguished from other terms.
2
Understand the definition
Identify the key components: al-waqt is the single part of duration that becomes distinguished by the occurrence of a specific known event.
The example of the rising of the crescent of Shawwal helps illustrate this.
3
Compare the three terms
Notice the conditions for each term: al-midda is the essence, al-zaman is measured by motion, al-waqt is defined by an event.
This shows how the same underlying duration can be viewed differently.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters in theological discussions about time and causality.
It helps avoid confusion when discussing temporal events and divine decree.

Source Text

واللفظ الثالث : الوقت : وهو الجزء المفرد الذي عرف امتيازه عن غيره بسبب حدوث حادث ، معين معلوم الوقوع . كقولك : أتيك وقت طلوع هلال شوال . فالمدة اسم لذات هذا الشيء ، والزمان اسم له بشرط صيرورته مقدراً بالحركة ، والوقت اسم له بشرط أن يصير جزء معين فيه معرفاً لحدوث حادث معلوم الوقوع .
And the third term: al-waqt (the time/instant): it is the single part that has become known for its distinction from others due to the occurrence of an event, specific and known of occurrence. Like your saying: 'I will come to you at the time of the rising of the crescent of Shawwal.' For al-midda (duration) is a name for the essence of this thing; al-zaman (time) is a name for it under the condition of its being measured by motion; and al-waqt is a name for it under the condition that it becomes a specific part in it that makes known the occurrence of a known event.

Commentary
This passage defines the term al-waqt (the time or instant). The author explains that al-waqt is the individual part (al-juzʾ al-mufrad) of duration that has become distinguished from other parts because of the occurrence of a specific event whose occurrence is known. An example: 'I will come to you at the time of the rising of the crescent of Shawwal.' Then the author reviews the relationship among the terms: al-midda (duration) is the name for the essence of the thing; al-zaman is the name for that same essence under the condition that it is measured by motion; and al-waqt is the name for it under the condition that it becomes a specific part of that duration that identifies the occurrence of a known event. Thus, the three terms refer to the same underlying continuous time, but with different conditions: one as pure essence, one as measured by motion, and one as a demarcated part signaling an event.

The Local Nature of Day and Night
Definition of al-nahar and al-layl with spherical earth implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of day and night as durations of sunrise and sunset, and why they differ across locations due to the earth's spherical shape.

Core Idea
Day and night are local, not universal; they depend on earth's sphericity.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah introduces two fundamental terms: al-nahar (day) and al-layl (night). The author defines them relative to the sun's rising and setting, then explains a key implication: because the earth is spherical, day and night are not the same everywhere at once. This sets the stage for deeper discussions about causality and the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definitions
Read the first sentence defining al-nahar and al-layl. Notice the concise style and the use of 'muddat' (duration) to emphasize time.
2
Understand the astronomical basis
The author states that the sun is always rising on one hemisphere and setting on the other. Because of the spherical earth, the conditions differ. This is an established fact in astronomy.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Thus, each location has its own day and night. This shows the relativity of these terms and guards against thinking day and night are universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللفظ الرابع : النهار : وهو مدة طلوع الشمس. والليل فهو مدة غروبها . ولما ثبت أن الشمس طالعة أبداً على أحد نصفي الأرض ، وغاربة عن النصف الثاني منها أبداً ، إلا أن أحوال طلوعها وغروبها بالنسبة إلى نصفي الأرض مختلفة ، بسبب كون الأرض كرة . لا جرم كان نهار كل موضع من الأرض غير نهار الموضع الآخر ، وليل كل موضع ، غير ليل الموضع الآخر

And since it is established that the sun is always rising on one hemisphere of the earth, and always setting on the other hemisphere, but the conditions of its rising and setting relative to the two hemispheres differ because the earth is spherical, inevitably the day of every place on earth is different from the day of another place, and the night of every place is different from the night of another place.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines the terms al-nahār (day) and al-layl (night). The author states that al-nahār is the duration of the sun's rising, and al-layl is the duration of its setting. Then he adds an astronomical observation: it is established that the sun is always rising on one hemisphere of the earth and always setting on the other, but because the earth is spherical, the circumstances of sunrise and sunset differ relative to each hemisphere. Consequently, the day at any given location is not the same as the day at another location, and similarly for the night. This highlights the relativity of day and night to geographic position, based on the earth's sphericity.

Al-Dahr and Al-Sarmad: Timeless Essence
Definition of al-dahr and al-sarmad as timeless names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-dahr (perpetuity) and al-sarmad (eternity) are terms referring to the essence when it is free from temporal events and changes, making them opposite to the concept of time.

Core Idea
Perpetuity and eternity are names for the essence free from change.

This short passage introduces two key terms in Islamic theology: al-dahr and al-sarmad. They are often confused with time, but the author clarifies that they denote the essence's timeless state. Let's read the definition carefully and see how they contrast with al-zaman (time).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and English
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: it first states the terms and then defines them by negation (free from temporal events).
2
Identify the two terms and their definition
Al-dahr and al-sarmad are explicitly identified as 'two names for the essence (jawhar) of this thing'. The definition hinges on the phrase 'free from accompanying temporal events and changes'.
3
Understand the contrast with time
The last sentence makes a direct contrast: the concept derived from these terms is the opposite of the concept of al-zaman (time). Time involves change and measurement, while these terms imply unchanging eternity.
This contrast is central to the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللفظ الخامس : الدهر والسرمد : فهما اسمان الجوهر هذا الشيء حال كونه خالياً عن مقارنة الحادثات والتغيرات. فصار المفهوم منها كالمضاد للمفهوم من الزمان
The fifth term: al-dahr (perpetuity) and al-sarmad (eternity): they are two names for the essence (al-jawhar) of this thing while it is free from accompanying temporal events and changes. So the concept derived from them has become like the opposite of the concept derived from al-zaman (time).
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines al-dahr (perpetuity) and al-sarmad (eternity). The author states that both are names for the essence (jawhar) of the thing when it is free from any accompaniment of temporal events (al-ḥādithāt) and changes (al-taghayyurāt). Thus, the concept derived from these terms is the opposite of the concept derived from al-zaman (time), which involves change and measurement. In other words, al-dahr and al-sarmad denote the unchanging, eternal nature of the essence, while al-zaman denotes its measured, temporal aspect.

Correction: The passage clarifies they are opposite to time because they describe the essence when it is free from events and changes, whereas time necessarily involves change.

The Terms al-Azal and al-Abad: Definitions and a Philosophical Problem
Definition of أزل and أبد, then a discussion on whether أزل has existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of al-azal and al-abad, and the philosophical problem regarding whether al-azal has actual existence.

Core Idea
The term al-azal is paradoxical: if it exists, it becomes temporal; if not, it seems meaningless.

In this passage, the author defines two key temporal terms in kalam: al-azal (pre-eternity) and al-abad (post-eternity). Then he raises a subtle problem: does the concept of al-azal have any real existence? He examines two possibilities, each leading to difficulty. This discussion sharpens our understanding of divine timelessness.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the first two lines defining al-azal and al-abad. Notice how al-azal is the 'advancing dahr without beginning' and al-abad is the 'receding dahr without end.'
The term dahr (دهر) means perpetuity or eternal duration.
2
Identify the investigation
The author then asks whether the thing named al-azal has realization and existence. This is the core problem of the passage.
3
Understand the rejection of the first possibility
The first possibility (that al-azal exists) is rejected because any existent time has determination and distinction, making it temporally ordered, which contradicts the nature of al-azal.
This shows that a temporal existence cannot be pre-eternal.
4
Note the problem with the second possibility
The second possibility (that al-azal does not exist) is also problematic, but the text cuts off. Consider why it might be problematic.
If it does not exist, how can we meaningfully speak of it?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللفظ السادس : الأزل : وهو الدهر المتقدم، الذي لا أول له . وأما الأيد فهو الدهر المتأخر الذي لا آخر له . وههنا بحث وهو أن مسمى الأزل هل له تحقق ووجود أم لا ؟ أما الأول : فهو باطل لأن كل وقت يكون موجوداً في نفسه فله تعين ، وامتياز عما عداه ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متأخر عما قبله ، ومتقدم على ما بعده ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو ضد الأزل ، ولا يصدق عليه كونه أزلياً . وأما الثاني فهو أيضاً مشكل . لأن مسمى الأزل ، إذا لم يكن له في
The sixth term: al-azal (pre-eternity): it is the advancing dahr (perpetuity) that has no beginning. And as for al-abad (post-eternity): it is the receding dahr that has no end. And here is an investigation: does the thing named al-azal have realization (taḥaqquq) and existence (wujūd) or not? As for the first: it is false, because every time (waqt) that exists in itself has determination (taʿayyun) and distinction (imtiyāz) from what is other than it, and everything that is like that is later than what precedes it and earlier than what comes after it, and everything that is like that is the opposite of al-azal, and its being eternal (azalī) is not true of it. And as for the second: it is also problematic, because the thing named al-azal, if it does not have in...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines the terms al-azal (pre-eternity) and al-abad (post-eternity). Al-azal is the advancing perpetuity without beginning; al-abad is the receding perpetuity without end. Then the author raises an investigation (baḥth): does the concept of al-azal actually have realization and existence? He considers two possibilities. The first possibility (that it does have existence) is false, because any time (waqt) that exists in itself has determination and distinction from others, and thus is temporally ordered (later than what precedes, earlier than what follows). Such temporal ordering is opposite to the notion of al-azal, which is supposed to have no beginning. Therefore, an existent time cannot be described as azalī (pre-eternal). The second possibility (that it does not have existence) is also problematic, but the text cuts off before explaining why. The author thus leaves the question open.

Key Terms
الأزل (al-azal)
الأبد (al-abad)
الدهر (al-dahr)
تحقق (taḥaqquq)
وجود (wujūd)
تعين (taʿayyun)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may assume that الأزل is a positive attribute of God, like an existing entity.
Correction: The author shows that if الأزل were an existing time, it would be temporally ordered, contradicting its definition. Thus, الأزل cannot be a real existent; it is a conceptual term indicating absence of beginning.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Night and Day Are Relative to Location
Definition of al-nahār and al-layl with proof of global variation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defines night and day and why they vary across the Earth due to its spherical shape.

Core Idea
Day and night are not universal; they depend on location and Earth's sphericity.

In this lesson, we examine a short but important passage where the author defines two everyday terms—night and day—and then proves that they are relative, not absolute. The argument relies on the spherical shape of the Earth, showing an early scientific awareness within Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style of the author.
The passage begins with 'al-lafth al-rābi‘' (the fourth term), indicating it is part of a series.
2
Identify the definitions
Find where the author defines 'al-nahār' and 'al-layl'. Note that they are defined in terms of the sun's rising and setting.
Definition: day = duration of sunrise; night = duration of sunset.
3
Understand the proof of variation
The author argues that because the sun is always rising on one hemisphere and setting on the other, and because the Earth is spherical, the exact times of sunrise and sunset differ for different places.
The key phrase is 'bi-sabab kawn al-arḍ kuratan' (due to the earth being a sphere).
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that the daytime of each place is different from that of any other place, and similarly for nighttime.
This refutes the idea that day and night are the same everywhere at the same time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللفظ الرابع : النهار : وهو مدة طلوع الشمس. والليل فهو مدة غروبها . ولما ثبت أن الشمس طالعة أبداً على أحد نصفي الأرض ، وغاربة عن النصف الثاني منها أبداً ، إلا أن أحوال طلوعها وغروبها بالنسبة إلى نصفي الأرض مختلفة ، بسبب كون الأرض كرة . لا جرم كان نهار كل موضع من الأرض غير نهار الموضع الآخر ، وليل كل موضع ، غير ليل الموضع الآخر
The fourth term: al-nahār (the day): it is the duration of the sun's rising. And al-layl (the night): it is the duration of its setting. And since it has been established that the sun is always rising over one of the two halves of the earth, and always setting over the other half, except that the states of its rising and setting differ with respect to the two halves due to the earth being a sphere, inevitably the daytime of each place on earth is other than the daytime of the other place, and the nighttime of each place is other than the nighttime of the other place.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines two terms: al-nahār (day) and al-layl (night). The author begins by stating that day is the period of the sun's rising, and night is the period of its setting. He then provides a proof that these periods vary across the globe. The proof is based on the fact that the sun is always rising on one hemisphere and setting on the other, but because the earth is spherical, the exact moments of sunrise and sunset differ for different locations. Consequently, the author concludes that the day and night of each place are distinct from those of any other place. This passage demonstrates an early understanding of spherical Earth and its implications for defining temporal periods.

الليل
طلوع الشمس

كرة الأرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The author shows that due to Earth's sphericity, day and night differ by location; one place's day is another's night.

Defining al-Dahr and al-Sarmad: Duration Without Change
The author defines al-dahr and al-sarmad as terms for duration devoid of temporal events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of al-dahr and al-sarmad as terms for duration devoid of events, and their conceptual opposition to time (al-zaman).

Core Idea
Al-dahr and al-sarmad name a duration free from events, opposite to time.

In this lesson, we explore two key terms from Islamic theology: al-dahr and al-sarmad. The author introduces them as the fifth pair of terms in his list, defining them as names for the essence of a thing when it is completely free from events and changes. This sets them apart from the common conception of time, which is tied to change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author presents these two terms together as a pair.
Pay attention to the phrase 'خالياً عن مقارنة الحادثات والتغيرات' – this is the core definition.
2
Understand the definition
The author says al-dahr and al-sarmad are names for the essence of something when it is devoid of events and changes. Reflect on what this means: a duration that is not measured by occurrences.
3
Contrast with time (al-zaman)
The author states that the concept from these terms is 'like opposite' to the concept from al-zaman. Why? Because time is usually associated with change and sequence. This contrast is central.
Try to think of a real-world example: time as we experience it is full of events; al-dahr/al-sarmad abstract that away.
4
Apply the concept
Consider how this distinction might be used in kalam arguments about eternity and creation. The terms appear in debates about the world's beginning and God's attributes.
This step connects to broader theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللفظ الخامس : الدهر والسرمد : فهما اسمان الجوهر هذا الشيء حال كونه خالياً عن مقارنة الحادثات والتغيرات. فصار المفهوم منها كالمضاد للمفهوم من الزمان
The fifth term: al-dahr (time/eternity) and al-sarmad (perpetuity): they are two names for the essence of this thing in the state of its being devoid of the accompaniment of events and changes. Thus the concept derived from them became as if opposed to the concept derived from al-zamān (time).
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines two related terms: al-dahr and al-sarmad. The author states that both are names for the essence or substance of a thing when it is free from the occurrence of events and changes. In other words, they refer to a duration that is not marked by temporal events. Consequently, the meaning of these terms is presented as opposite to the meaning of al-zamān (time), which is typically associated with change and measurement. This is a technical distinction in kalam philosophy regarding types of duration.

السرمد
الزمان

التغيرات

D) They are unrelated

Key Takeaway
Al-dahr and al-sarmad denote a duration entirely free from events and changes, conceptually opposite to time as commonly understood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says that al-dahr and al-sarmad describe a thing when it is free from events and changes. Think about your own soul: if every life event—every joy, every sorrow, every accomplishment—were stripped away, what would remain of you? Does that thought unsettle you or give you peace? Why?
?
If time is tied to change, and al-dahr is its opposite, then what part of your relationship with Allah can be said to belong to al-dahr? Have you ever experienced a moment of worship so still that time seemed to vanish? What keeps you from living more of your life in that state?
?
This lesson teaches that al-dahr and al-sarmad are names for the essence of a thing when devoid of change. If you truly believe that Allah knows you in that unchanging essence—the 'you' before and beyond all events—how does that change the way you view your worth? Does it make you more or less attached to the changing circumstances of your life?
