Al-Azal and Al-Abad: The Reality of Beginningless Eternity
Author defines al-azal and al-abad and debates their reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of al-azal and al-abad, and the philosophical problem regarding the reality of beginninglessness.

Core Idea
The concept of 'no beginning' must be real, yet its true essence is beyond human grasp.

In this passage, the author defines two key terms: al-azal (eternity without beginning) and al-abad (eternity without end). He then delves into a profound discussion about whether the concept of al-azal has any actual existence. This lesson unpacks his arguments and final position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text carefully, then its English translation. Notice the terms for 'eternity without beginning' and 'eternity without end'.
The passage uses 'dahr' (duration) to explain these concepts.
2
Identify the two positions on al-azal's reality
The author presents two views: that al-azal has realization (exists) or that it does not. He critiques both.
3
Understand the reasoning against each position
Against the first position: anything that exists in itself has specification and is temporal, contradicting al-azal. Against the second: denying its reality leads to impossibility because we must acknowledge beginninglessness somehow.
The author uses logical arguments here.
4
Grasp the author's conclusion and its nuance
The author concludes that beginninglessness must be acknowledged either in existence or non-existence, but its true conception is beyond human intellects. Reflect on this nuanced position.
This shows the limits of human reason in grasping divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللفظ السادس : الأزل : وهو الدهر المتقدم، الذي لا أول له . وأما الأيد فهو الدهر المتأخر الذي لا آخـر لـه . وههنا بحث وهو أن مسمى الأزل هل له تحقق ووجود أم لا ؟ أما الأول : فهو باطل لأن كل وقت يكون موجوداً في نفسه فله تعين ، وامتياز عما عداه ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متأخر عما قبله ، ومتقدم على ما بعده ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو ضد الأزل ، ولا يصدق عليه كونه أزلياً . وأما الثاني فهو أيضاً مشكل . لأن مسمى الأزل ، إذا لم يكن له في نفسه تحقق فذلك محال . لأنه لا بد من الاعتراف بتحقق اللاأولية ، إما في وجود العالم أو في عدمه . واعلم أن الحق أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بتحقق اللاأولية إما في الوجود أو في عدمها . إلا أن الحق أن تصوره من حيث إنه هو ، أعلى شأناً من العقول البشرية ، والأفكار الإنسانية
The sixth term: al-azal (eternity without beginning): it is the preceding dahr (duration) that has no beginning. As for al-abad (eternity without end), it is the subsequent dahr that has no end. And here is a discussion: whether the referent of al-azal has realization and existence or not? As for the first [view that it does have realization]: it is false, because every time that exists in itself has specification and distinction from what is other than it, and everything that is like that is subsequent to what is before it and prior to what is after it, and everything that is like that is opposite to al-azal, and it is not true of it that it is azalī (beginningless). As for the second [view that it does not have realization]: it is also problematic. Because if the referent of al-azal has no realization in itself, that is impossible. Because it is necessary to acknowledge the reality of 'having no beginning' (al-lā-awwalīyya), either in the existence of the world or in its non-existence. And know that the truth is that it is necessary to acknowledge the reality of 'having no beginning' either in existence or in its non-existence. Except that the truth is that its conception in itself is higher than human intellects and human thoughts.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines al-azal and al-abad. Al-azal is the eternal duration that has no beginning, while al-abad is the eternal duration that has no end. The author then raises a crucial problem: does the concept of al-azal actually have any reality or existence? He examines two opposing positions. The first position, which affirms that al-azal has existence, is rejected. The reasoning is that anything that exists in itself is a specific, distinct entity; such an entity is necessarily preceded by something else and succeeded by something else, making it temporal and thus opposite to the meaning of al-azal. Therefore, calling it 'beginningless' would be false. The second position, which denies that al-azal has any reality, is also problematic. If al-azal has no existence at all, then we cannot admit the reality of 'beginninglessness' (al-lā-awwalīyya) either in the existence of the world or in its non-existence, which is impossible because we must acknowledge that the world either existed from eternity or was preceded by nothingness. The author then declares the truth: we must indeed acknowledge the reality of beginninglessness, either in existence (i.e., the world exists eternally) or in non-existence (i.e., the world was preceded by eternal non-existence). However, he adds that the true comprehension of al-azal in itself is beyond the capacity of human intellects and thoughts. This reflects a nuanced position that admits the necessity of the concept while recognizing its ineffability.

الأيد
الدهر
اللاأولية
تحقق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that al-azal is a fully comprehensible concept that can be grasped by the human mind.
Correction: The author states that the true conception of al-azal 'is higher than human intellects and human thoughts,' indicating its ineffability.

Checkpoint
What is the author's final conclusion about the reality of 'beginninglessness' (al-lā-awwalīyya)?

B) It must be acknowledged either in existence or non-existence

D) It is purely imaginary

Key Takeaway
The reality of 'beginninglessness' must be acknowledged, but its true nature is beyond human comprehension.

The Two Main Opinions on the Nature of Place
Author defines place and presents two main opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main rational opinions on the nature of place (al-makan) as discussed in Islamic kalam.

Core Idea
Place is either a void that bodies occupy or the inner surface of a container.

The author begins by observing that bodies move from one location to another, an undeniable fact. He then refines the concept of 'place' into two competing definitions: one that sees place as a penetrable dimension (void) and another that sees it as the containing surface. This passage sets the stage for deeper theological discussions about space, bodies, and the world's relationship to its Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Let the author's logical flow sink in before analyzing.
Pay attention to the list of synonyms for place at the beginning.
2
Identify the two definitions of place
The author presents two possibilities: place as something the body penetrates (dimension/void) or place as the inner surface of the containing body. Locate these in the text.
3
Note the proponents of each view
The first view is attributed to Plato and most rational people. The second view is not attributed to a specific figure here but is presented as the alternative.
The author does not name supporters of the second view in this passage.
4
Reflect on the exclusivity of the two opinions
The author concludes that these are the only two refined rational opinions. Think about why he considers all other possibilities reducible to these two.
This is a typical kalam move: reducing multiplicity to a logical dichotomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في تفصيل مذاهب الناس فيه اعلم أنا نشاهد أن الجسم ينتقل ويتحرك ، ونعلم بالبديهة أنه ينتقل من جهة إلى جهة، ومن جانب إلى جانب . والناس يسمون المنتقل عنه والمنتقل إليه تارة بالحيز ، وتارة بالجهة ، وتارة بالمحاذاة (۱) وتارة بالجنب ، وتارة بالجانب ، وتارة بالمكان ، وتارة بأسماء أخرى، لا حاجة لنا إلى الاستقصاء في ذكرها . وبالجملة فمكان الشيء هو الذي يكون فيه الشيء ، ويفارقه بالحركة ولا يسعه معه غيره وتتوارد المتحركات عليه على سبيل البدل . فهذا القدر أمر معلوم بالضرورة . ثم نقول : إن هذا الشيء إما أن يكون أمراً ينفذ فيه ذات الجسم ويسري فيه ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، بل يكون هو السطح الباطن من الجسم الحاوي ، المماس للسطح الظاهر من الجسم المحوي، والأول : هو القول بأن المكان هو البعد ، والفضاء ، وهو مذهب أفلاطون وأكثر العقلاء ، والثاني : هو القول بأن المكان هو السطح الحاوي. فالمذهب المحصل المعقول في المكان والحيز ليس إلا هذان القولان .
The first chapter: detailing the opinions of people concerning it. Know that we observe that the body moves and is in motion, and we know by intuition that it moves from one direction to another, from one side to another. People call that from which it moves and that to which it moves sometimes 'space', sometimes 'direction', sometimes 'alignment', sometimes 'side', sometimes 'aspect', sometimes 'place', and sometimes by other names — we have no need to enumerate them. In sum, the place of a thing is that in which the thing is, and it departs from it by motion, and nothing else can contain it along with it, and moving things succeed one another in it. This much is known necessarily. Then we say: This thing is either something in which the body's essence penetrates and permeates, or it is not like that, but rather it is the inner surface of the containing body that touches the outer surface of the contained body. The first is the opinion that place is dimension and void, which is the doctrine of Plato and most rational people. The second is the opinion that place is the containing surface. So the refined, rational opinion concerning place and space is nothing other than these two opinions.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage classifies the different names for place and then defines place as that in which a body is located. It first notes the empirical observation of motion and the intuitive knowledge of place as that which a body leaves and enters. Then it presents the theoretical question: is place something that the body penetrates (a void/dimension) or something that contains it (a surface)? The author identifies the two main schools: Plato and most rationalists hold that place is dimension/void (al-bu'd wa al-fada'), while others hold that place is the inner surface of the containing body. The passage concludes that these are the only two refined, rational opinions on the matter.

Correction: The author shows that place is either a penetrable dimension (void) or the inner surface of the container, not merely the volume.

Why Plato Called Place 'Prime Matter' and 'Form'
Author explains and defends Plato's symbolic naming of place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the symbolic interpretation behind Plato's statements that place is prime matter and form, and why literal criticism is invalid.

Core Idea
Plato's terms for place were symbolic, rooted in shared properties, not literal identifications.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliya clarifies Plato's doctrine of place. Some critics accused Plato of absurdity by taking his words literally. The author explains that Plato called place 'prime matter' and 'form' metaphorically, based on functional analogies, and argues that rational people should not judge such symbolic expressions literally.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the author's careful distinction between literal and metaphorical meaning.
2
Identify the two analogies
Find why place is called prime matter (stationary substrate) and why form (abstract dimensions). The author gives clear reasons for each.
These analogies are key to understanding Plato's intent.
3
Examine the refutation
The author addresses literalist objections: body moves with its parts, so place cannot be matter/form. Note how the author defends Plato by insisting on symbolic interpretation.
The author concludes that only void or surface are valid opinions on place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : القائلون بأن المكان هو البعد والفضاء . ثارة يسمونه بالهيولى ، وتارة يسمونه بالصورة. وإنما سموه بالهيون من حيث إن خاصية الهيولى أن تكون ذاته باقية ، وتكون ذاتها مورداً للأحوال المتعاقبة، والصفات المتلاحقة . وههنا هذا الفضاء أمر واقف لا يتغير ولا يتبدل البتة إنه تتوارد عليه الأجسام، وهذا الفضاء يقبلها ، فكأن هذا الفضاء مشابهاً للهيولى من هذا الوجه ، فلا جرم سموا هذا الفضاء بالهيولى بحسب هذا التأويل . وإنما سموه بالصورة ، وذلك لأن الجوهر الجسماني إنما امتاز عن الجواهر المجردة العقلية لأجل كونه قابلاً لهذه الأبعاد الثلاثة ، فهذه الأبعاد الثلاثة (۱)] هي كالجزء الصوري لماهية الجسم. فلما كان هذا الفضاء عبارة عن هذه الأبعاد المجردة الواقعة سموه بالصورة. بحسب هذا التأويل . فهذا هو التفسير الصحيح لما نقل عن أفلاطون ، أنه كان تارة يقول : المكان هو الهيولى ، وتارة كان يقول : المكان هو الصورة ثم إن جماعة ممن أرادوا تقبيح قوله في أعين الناس ، نقلوا عنه : أنه يقول : المكان عبارة عن الهيولي أو عن الصورة ، ثم أخذوا يحتجون على إبطاله بأن الجسم إذا انتقل من مكان إلى مكان ، فهو إنما انتقل بمجموع أجزائه التي هي الهيولى والصورة، فكيف يمكن [ أن يقال (۲) ] المكان هو الهيولى والصورة ؟ إلا أنه على الوجه الذي لخصناه ، ظهر أنه إنما أطلق اسم الهيولى تارة ، واسم الصورة أخرى على المكان. بناء على التأويل الذي شرحناه ، والتفسير الذي الخصناه [ فأما إجراء الكلام الذي قصد به الرمز على ظاهره ، ثم الاشتغال بالطعن فيه ، فذلك مما لا يليق بالعقلاء الكاملين ] (۳) وبالجملة : فقد ظهر بالبيان الذي لخصناه : أن القول المعتبر في حقيقة المكان هو أنه إما الفضاء . وإما السطح .
When you know this, we say: Those who say that place is dimension and void sometimes call it prime matter (hayula), and sometimes call it form (surah). They call it prime matter because the property of prime matter is that its essence remains and its essence is a substrate for successive states and consecutive attributes. Here, this void is something stationary that does not change or alter at all, yet bodies succeed one another in it and it receives them. So this void resembles prime matter from this aspect; therefore they call this void prime matter according to this interpretation. They call it form because the corporeal substance is distinguished from immaterial intellectual substances only by being receptive to these three dimensions. So these three dimensions are like the formal part of the essence of body. Since this void is these abstract, static dimensions, they call it form according to this interpretation. This is the correct interpretation of what is transmitted from Plato, that he sometimes said 'place is prime matter' and sometimes said 'place is form'. Then a group of those who wanted to discredit his statement in people's eyes transmitted from him that he says place is prime matter or form, and then they began to argue for its invalidity by saying: When a body moves from place to place, it moves with the totality of its parts which are prime matter and form; so how can it be said that place is prime matter and form? However, according to the manner we have summarized, it becomes clear that he applied the name prime matter sometimes and the name form other times to place, based on the interpretation we explained and the explanation we summarized. As for taking the speech that was intended as symbolism at face value and then occupying oneself with criticizing it, that is not befitting of perfect rational people. In sum, it has become clear through the demonstration we summarized that the valid opinion concerning the reality of place is that it is either void or surface.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage explains and defends Plato's apparent contradictory statements about place. The author first notes that those who hold place to be dimension/void (i.e., followers of Plato) sometimes call it prime matter (hayula) and sometimes form (surah). The reason for calling it prime matter is that prime matter is a permanent substrate that receives successive attributes; similarly, void is a static entity that receives successive bodies. The reason for calling it form is that body is defined by its receptivity to three dimensions, and void consists of those abstract dimensions, thus resembling form. The author stresses that this is the correct interpretation of Plato's words. Then the passage refutes critics who took Plato's statements literally and argued that since body moves with its parts (prime matter and form), place cannot be prime matter and form. The author argues that Plato's terms were metaphorical, not literal, and that criticizing without understanding the intended symbolism is unbecoming of rational people. The passage concludes by reiterating that the only valid opinions on place are void or surface.

Key Terms
هيولى
صورة
الأبعاد الثلاثة
تأويل
فضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Plato literally identified place with prime matter and form.
Correction: Plato's statements are symbolic: place shares properties with prime matter (being a substrate) and form (being abstract dimensions), not literal identity.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why did Plato call place 'form' (surah)?
A) Because place is the shape of the body
B) Because place consists of the three dimensions that are the formal part of body's essence
C) Because place is the cause of motion
D) Because place is the surface of the body

Key Takeaway
Plato's descriptions of place as prime matter and form are metaphorical, based on functional similarities, and must be interpreted symbolically rather than literally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a scholar's statement because you took it literally, without understanding their intended meaning? Will you now seek tafsir before judging?
?
When you encounter a difficult concept in Islam, do you rush to criticize or do you try to understand the underlying reason? Commit to a specific practice of seeking interpretation for ambiguous statements.
?
The author criticizes those who discredit Plato's words in people's eyes. Have you ever misrepresented someone's view to make them look foolish? How can you guard your tongue when discussing differing opinions?

Classification of Opinions on Space and Void
The author classifies the views of theologians and philosophers on the nature of space and void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main positions on the nature of space and void as classified by the author: the theologians' denial, the philosophers' affirmation with internal disagreement, and the Aristotelian definition of place.

Core Idea
Space and void: mere nothing, existing dimensions, or only a containing surface.

Before diving into arguments about space and void, it is essential to map out the existing views. The author presents a clear classification of opinions from theologians, philosophers, and Aristotelians. This classification sets the stage for later discussions on the nature of place and vacuum. Read carefully to see how each school defines space or void differently.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the classification structure become clear.
Notice the author uses 'الفرقة الثانية' to introduce the philosophers.
2
Identify the three main positions
The passage presents three distinct views. Locate each one: the theologians, the philosophers, and those who say place is the containing surface.
The philosophers are further subdivided into two groups.
3
Note the subdivision among philosophers
Within the philosophers, there are two opinions: one allows void without bodies (vacuum possible), the other says it's impossible. Understand the difference.
This subdivision is crucial for understanding later debates.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about why these distinctions matter. How does each view affect our understanding of physical reality and the existence of empty space?
This connects to broader topics in natural philosophy and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتكلمون فإنهم يقولون: هذا الفضاء وهذا الخلاء محض وليس له وجود البتة. والفرقة الثانية: الفلاسفة. وهم يقولون: هذا الخلاء أبعاد موجودة أمكنة للأجسام. وهذا القول هو اختيار أفلاطون الإلهي وأكثر من تقدمه من الحكماء المعتبرين. وهؤلاء فريقان: منهم من يقول: لا امتناع في بقاء هذا الفضاء خالياً عن الأجسام، ومنهم من يقول: إن ذلك ممتنع. وأما القائلون بأن المكان هو السطح الحاوي فقط. فهذا قول أرسطاطاليس وجمهور أتباعه كأبي نصر الفارابي، وأبي علي بن سينا. فهذا تفصيل المذاهب في هذا الباب.
The theologians (al-mutakallimūn) say: This space (al-faḍāʾ) and this void (al-khalāʾ) are mere [nothing] and have no existence at all. And the second group: the philosophers (al-falāsifah). And they say: This void is existing dimensions, places for bodies. And this opinion is the choice of the divine Plato and most of the respected sages who preceded him. And these are two groups: among them are those who say: There is no impossibility in this space remaining empty of bodies; and among them are those who say: That is impossible. And as for those who say that place (al-makān) is only the containing surface (al-saṭḥ al-ḥāwī)—this is the opinion of Aristotle and the majority of his followers, such as Abū Naṣr al-Fārābī and Abū ʿAlī ibn Sīnā. So this is the detailed account of the schools on this topic.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a classification of opinions on the nature of space and void. It begins by stating the view of the mutakallimun (theologians) who deny any real existence to space or void, considering them mere nothingness. Then it presents the second group, the falasifah (philosophers), who affirm that void exists as dimensions serving as places for bodies. This view is attributed to Plato and earlier wise men. The author further divides the philosophers into two subgroups: those who hold that space can remain empty of bodies (i.e., a vacuum is possible) and those who hold that it is impossible for space to be empty (i.e., it must always contain a body). Finally, it mentions a third position: those who define place as the containing surface only, which is the view of Aristotle and his followers like al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā. The passage concludes by stating that this is the complete summary of the schools on this issue.

Correction: The philosophers are split into two camps: some allow a vacuum, others deny it.

Claim and First Proof: Void Has Measurable Parts
Author states his claim and gives first proof: void has measurable parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's claim that void has magnitude and the first proof from intuitive comparison showing void cannot be pure nothing.

Core Idea
If void has half and third, it is measured; pure nothing cannot be measured.

Imagine a void the size of one cubit compared to two cubits — intuitively, the first is half of the second. This simple observation forms the core of a powerful argument against those who claim void is pure nothing. The author uses this to show that void, if it existed, would be existent and measurable, not absolute non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the logical flow from claim to proof.
The passage begins with the author's stance.
2
Identify the claim and the proof
Find where the author states his claim about void being existent with magnitude. Then locate the first proof that uses intuitive comparison of sizes.
3
Analyze the key phrase
Focus on 'كل ما له نصف وثلث وربع يكون ممسوحاً مقدراً'. This is the crucial logical link: having fractions implies measurability, which cannot apply to pure nothing.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why the author concludes that void cannot be pure nothing. Connect this to the broader topic of refuting those who deny physical existence of void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في ابطال قول من يقول : الخلاء والفضا، عدم محض ونفي صرف اعلم أنا ندعي أن هذا الخلاء لو حصل لكان موجوداً ، له مقدار وامتداد في الجهات، ولم يكن عدماً محضاً ، ونفياً صرفاً . كما يقوله المتكلمون . والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنا نعلم بالبديهة أن الخلاء الذي يكون بمقدار ذراع، نصف الخلاء الذي يكون بمقدار ذراعين، وثلث ما يكون بمقدار ثلاثة أذرع، وربع ما يكون بمقدار أربعة أذرع. وكل ما يكون له نصف وثلث وربع يكون ممسوحاً مقدراً، فإنه لا يكون نفياً محضاً وعدماً صرفاً. فإن من المعلوم بالضرورة أن العدم المحض لا يكون له نصف وثلث وربع ولا يكون موصوفاً بالأقل والأكثر والزائد والناقص والمساحة والتقدير.
The second chapter: On invalidating the statement of those who say: Void and space are pure nothing and absolute non-existence. Know that we claim that this void, if it were to occur, would be existent, having magnitude and extension in directions, and would not be pure nothing and absolute non-existence, as the theologians (mutakallimūn) say. And what indicates it are [several] aspects: The first: We know by intuition (badīha) that a void that is a cubit in size is half of a void that is two cubits in size, and a third of what is three cubits in size, and a quarter of what is four cubits in size. And everything that has a half, a third, and a quarter is measured and quantified, so it is not pure nothing and absolute non-existence. For it is known necessarily (ḍarūra) that pure nothing does not have a half, a third, or a quarter, nor is it described as less and more, increasing and decreasing, and measurement and quantification.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the author's claim and his first proof. He begins by stating the chapter's purpose: to refute those who say void and space are pure nothing. Then he asserts his claim: if void existed, it would be existent with magnitude and extension, not absolute nothing. He then presents the first proof: we know intuitively that a one-cubit void is half of a two-cubit void, etc. Having a half, third, and quarter implies measurement and quantification. Pure nothing cannot have these properties. Therefore, void cannot be pure nothing. The reasoning is a clear argument from intuitive comparison.

The Void is Perceptible by the Senses
Second proof: void is perceptible by the senses and indicated by spatial reference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the existence of void (space) based on sensory indication and the implication of magnitude and length.

Core Idea
We can point to void and describe its length, so it cannot be pure nothing.

The second proof in the series argues for the existence of void by appealing to everyday sensory experience. When we say 'from here to there,' we are referring to a spatial magnitude that must be real. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows why void is not mere non-existence.

Guided Path

3

Some argue void is not seen, so it might be nothing. But the author points out that sensory indication includes spatial reference, not just vision. How does this counter the misunderstanding?
Refer to the common misunderstanding provided.
4
Connect to the overall argument
This proof supports the existence of void. Consider how this connects to the earlier proof (if known) or how it stands alone.

Thus, the void is existent.

Third Proof: Bodies Enter and Leave Void
Third proof: bodies entering and leaving void implies void's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the third proof argues that void must be something real rather than pure nothing.

Core Idea
If void were nothing, bodies could not enter or leave it.

This lesson presents the third argument for the existence of void. The author observes that we commonly speak of a body being in a void and then moving to another void. But if the void were pure nothing, such statements would be meaningless. The proof shows that rational judgment about bodies in space forces us to accept void as something existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author begins with a common observation about bodies and voids.
Pay attention to the logical flow from observation to conclusion.
2
Identify the key claim
Identify the claim that void cannot be pure nothing because bodies are judged to be in it and move from it.
The judgment itself implies void has some reality.
3
Understand the underlying reason
The author explains why: it is inconceivable for a body to be 'in nothing' or to move 'from nothing to nothing'.
Think about why 'in nothing' is problematic.
4
Reflect on the proof's strength
Consider how this proof relies on the intelligibility of spatial relations. If we can meaningfully say a body is 'in' a void, the void cannot be absolutely nonexistent.
This is a rational argument, not empirical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن هذا الفضاء، وهذا الخلاء، يحكم عليه بأن الجسم حصل فيه. ثم يقال: خرج ذلك الجسم عنه. وانتقل إلى خلاء آخر. والمحكوم عليه بأنه خلاء للجسم ومفر له، وبأن الجسم قد حصل فيه تارة، وانتقل عنه أخرى. كيف يكون عدماً محضاً ونفياً صرفاً؟ فإن حصول الجسم في العدم المحض غير معقول، وانتقاله من عدم إلى عدم آخر غير معقول.
The third [aspect]: This space (al-faḍā') and this void (al-khalā') are judged that a body (al-jism) occurs in it. Then it is said: That body came out of it and moved to another void. And that which is judged to be a void for the body and a space for it, and that the body sometimes occurs in it and at other times moves away from it—how can it be pure nothing and absolute non-existence? For the occurrence of a body in pure nothing is inconceivable, and its transition from one nothing to another nothing is inconceivable.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof. The author notes that we commonly say that a body is in a void, then leaves it and goes to another void. This presupposes that the void is something that can contain the body. If the void were pure nothing, it would be impossible for a body to be located in it, because 'being in nothing' is unintelligible. Similarly, moving from one nothing to another is inconceivable. Therefore, void must be existent.

Correction: The proof does not assume void exists; it starts from the fact that we make true judgments about bodies in void, and argues that such judgments would be false if void were pure nothing.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that void must be something real because bodies cannot intelligibly be said to occupy or move through pure nothing.

The Fourth Proof: Shared Boundary of Voids
Fourth proof: shared boundary indicates existence of void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of a shared boundary (al-faṣl al-mushtarak) between voids demonstrates that void is existent with magnitude.

Core Idea
The boundary between two voids proves both are real and extended.

This lesson presents the fourth proof for the existence of void, based on the logic of shared boundaries. The author draws a powerful analogy with surfaces to show that the very language we use to indicate location implies real existence. By examining how we speak of 'here' and 'there' in relation to void, we uncover a rational argument for its reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the repetition of the phrases 'here and there' (ههنا وهناك).
This is a dense argument—take your time.
2
Identify the analogy
The author compares void to a surface. Under the structure: just as 'here and there' for a surface indicate a boundary that proves the surface exists, so too for void.
The analogy is explicit: 'just as... so likewise'.
3
Grasp the logical move
The key is that the 'common boundary' (الفصل المشترك) is applied to void in the same way as to a surface. Since it indicates existence for a surface, it must for void.
This is a qiyas (analogical reasoning) in kalam.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion: void is existent, having magnitude and extension. Think about what magnitude means for a void—it implies real dimensions.
This refutes the idea that void is merely nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنا إذا قلنا : الخلاء الذي من ههنا إلى هناك . فقولنا : هنا وهناك ، فصل مشترك بينه وبين الخلاء الذي يكون خارجاً عنه ، كما أنا إذا قلنا : هذا السطح من ههنا إلى هناك . فإن قولنا : ههنا وهناك ، إشارة إلى الفصل المشترك الذي بين هذا السطح، وبين ذلك السطح الخارج عنه، المتصل به ، وكما أن إيقاع الفعل المشترك في السطح يدل قطعاً على كون ذلك السطح أمراً موجوداً ، فكذلك إيقاع الفصل المشترك في الخلاء ، وجب أن يدل قطعاً على كون ذلك الخلاء : موجوداً له مقدار وامتداد في الجهات.
The fourth [aspect]: When we say ‘the void that is from here to there,’ our saying ‘here and there’ is a common boundary (faṣl mushtarak) between it and the void that is outside it, just as when we say ‘this surface from here to there,’ our saying ‘here and there’ is an indication of the common boundary between this surface and that external surface connected to it. And just as the application of the common term to the surface necessarily indicates that the surface is an existent thing, so likewise the application of the common term to the void necessarily indicates that the void is existent, having magnitude and extension in directions.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, based on the logic of shared boundaries. When we say 'the void from here to there,' the words 'here' and 'there' indicate a common boundary between that void and another void outside it. The author draws an analogy with surfaces: when we say 'this surface from here to there,' 'here' and 'there' refer to the boundary between that surface and an adjacent surface. Since the latter implies the surface exists, the former similarly implies the void exists. Thus void is existent with magnitude.

Key Terms
الفصل المشترك (al-faṣl al-mushtarak)
السطح (al-saṭḥ)
الإشارة (al-ishāra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The shared boundary is merely a mental concept with no real counterpart.
Correction: The author argues that the application of the common boundary label to void logically necessitates that void is an existent thing, just as with surfaces.

Checkpoint
In the fourth proof, what analogy does the author use to argue for the existence of void?
A) The analogy of a surface and its boundary.
B) The analogy of a line and a point.
C) The analogy of time and space.
D) The analogy of a body and its parts.

Key Takeaway
The shared boundary between two voids rationally demonstrates that void is an existent thing with magnitude and extension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth about the unseen (like the existence of angels or the afterlife) simply because it lacked physical boundaries? How does this proof challenge that tendency by showing that boundaries themselves point to reality?

The author uses an analogy from physical surfaces to argue for something intangible. In your own life, when are you tempted to reject an invisible reality despite clear analogies from the visible world? What would it take for you to accept such a reality?
?
The shared boundary exists only because both voids exist. Can you think of a relationship in your life where the presence of a 'boundary' (e.g., a disagreement or a shared experience) proves the reality of both sides? How does that affect your approach to that relationship?

Fifth Proof: Relational Properties of Voids
Fifth proof: diversity and relations of voids show they are real.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof for the reality of void based on the relational properties assigned to it by the mutakallimūn.

Core Idea
If void were pure nothing, it could not be described as near, far, connected, or separate.

This passage presents the fifth proof from the mutakallimūn's own language about motion and locations. They naturally describe voids as having properties like distance and connection. Such descriptions would be impossible if the void were nothing. This shows that void must be a real entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text from 'الخامس' to 'هذه الأحوال' without translation first. Notice the technical terms.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the four relational properties
Find the words for connected (متصلة), separate (منفصلة), near (متقاربة), and distant (متباعدة). Understand how they are used.
3
Trace the logical progression
Follow the argument: motion definition → intermediaries → classification → distance example → conclusion.
This is a proof chain.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
The passage ends by asking how pure nothing could have such states. Answer the question in your own words.
This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إن المتكلمين يحكمون عليها بأنها أمور متغايرة بالعدد ، ومتباينة بالشخص . فإنهم يقولون : الحركة عبارة عن خروج الجسم من الحيز الأول ودخوله في الحيز الثاني، وزعموا أنه ليس بين الحيز الأول وبين الحيز الثاني واسطة أصلاً ، وأنه حصل بين الحيز الأول وبين الحيز الثالث واسطة . فحكموا على بعض هذه الأحياز بأنها متصلة وعلى بعضها بأنها منفصلة ، وعلى بعضها بأنها متقاربة ، وعلى بعضها بأنها متباعدة، فإن البعد بين الحيز المعين وبين الحيز الخامس منه أقل من البعد بين الحيز المعين وبين الحيز العاشر منه. وما كان عدماً محضاً فكيف يعقل وصفه بهذه الأحوال ؟
The fifth [aspect]: The theologians (mutakallimūn) judge them to be things differing in number and distinct in individual. For they say: Motion is the expression of the body's exit from the first location (al-ḥayyiz al-awwal) and its entry into the second location, and they claimed that there is no intermediary at all between the first location and the second, and that there occurred an intermediary between the first location and the third location. Thus they judged some of these locations to be connected, some to be separate, some to be near, and some to be distant. For the distance between a given location and the fifth from it is less than the distance between that same location and the tenth from it. And how can that which is pure nothing be conceived to be described by these states?
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof, drawing on the theologians' (mutakallimūn) language about motion and locations. They talk about a body leaving one location (ḥayyiz) and entering another. They also discuss intermediaries between locations. Locations are described as connected, separate, near, or far. For example, the distance to the fifth location is less than to the tenth. Such relational properties would be impossible for pure nothing. Therefore, these locations (voids) must be real existents.

Key Terms
المتكلمون
الحركة
الحيز
المتباعدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think locations are just abstract concepts with no real existence.
Correction: The argument shows that locations have concrete relational properties like distance and connection, which are real, so locations must be real.

Checkpoint
What kind of properties does the author use in the fifth proof to argue that void is real?
A) Color and shape.
B) Connection, separation, nearness, and distance.
C) Weight and density.
D) Time and duration.

Key Takeaway
The void must be real because pure nothing cannot have relational properties like distance, connection, or separation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often treat your spiritual failures as mere absences—'I just didn't pray'—as if they were nothing. But this lesson shows that even a void has real properties and effects. What are the real consequences of your neglect that you have been dismissing as 'nothing'? Will you own them tonight and repent with full awareness?
?
This proof forces you to see that relational properties require a real subject. Look at your relationships: your distance from Allah, from family, from truth. Are you treating that distance as an abstract concept or as a real, measurable void that must be filled? How will you act to reduce that distance starting tomorrow?
?
You rely on logic to affirm the reality of voids—so why do you rely on feelings to doubt Allah's existence? The same mind that accepts this proof can accept the proof for the Creator. What is holding you back from applying the same rigorous reasoning to your faith? Make a concrete step to examine one proof for God's existence today.

Voids Have Directional Properties
Sixth proof (incomplete): voids have different directional attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth proof's claim that voids possess directional attributes, suggesting their existence.

Core Idea
If void had no existence, it could not have properties like above and below.

The sixth proof argues from the observable fact that different void locations are described by distinct directional attributes such as 'above' and 'below'. This diversity of properties is evidence that the void must be a real existent, not pure nothing. In this lesson, we examine the opening of the proof and its underlying logic.

Guided Path
1

2
Identify the core premise
The author states that void locations are described by different attributes (above, below, right, left). This is the premise of the proof.
These are directional properties that nothingness cannot have.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why these directional properties imply existence. If void were truly nothing, it could not have such diverse attributes. The proof is cut off, but the conclusion would be that void must exist.
This is an argument from the reality of properties to the reality of the substance.

The Opponent's Proof that Places Must Be Existent
The interlocutor argues that spatial directions cannot be pure non-existence, so places must be real.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's logical argument for why spatial places (al-ahiyaz) must be real, existent entities, and why that argument cannot be dismissed as mere negation.

Core Idea
Directions like 'above' and 'below' cannot be pure negations—else they'd be existent by double negation.

This passage presents a philosophical argument from an opponent (likely a natural philosopher) that places (ahiyaz) must be real existents. The argument uses the example of spatial directions 'above' and 'below' to show that they cannot be mere negations of each other. Understanding this argument is crucial because it sets up the kalam response that will follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Note the logical structure: the opponent gives two lines of reasoning.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ليس عدماً محضاً' (not pure non-existence) at the start.
2
Trace the double-negation argument
Follow why making each direction the negation of the other leads to each being a 'non-existence of non-existence,' which would be existent.
This is a parity argument: there is no reason to privilege one over the other.
3
Consider the alternative attribution argument
If directions are existent attributes, then the places they describe must also be existent, because an attribute cannot inhere in nothingness.
This second route reinforces the same conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحت ليس عدماً محضاً ، لأنه ليس جعل الفوقية عبارة عن عدم التحتية ، أولى من العكس . فإن جعلنا كل واحد منها عدماً للآخر ، مع أن كل واحد منهما في نفسه عدم ، كان كل واحد منهما عدماً للعدم ، فيكون كل واحد منهما أمراً موجوداً [ وإن جعلنا كل واحد منهما وصفاً موجوداً ] فحينئذ يلزم أن تكون هذه الأحياز الموصوفة بهذه الصفات الموجودة [ موجودة ] لامتناع قيام الصفة الموجودة بالنفي المحض والعدم الصرف. فهذه الدلائل وأمثالها دلائل ظاهرة جلية في إثبات أن هذه الأحياز لا بد وأن تكون موجودة.
And "below" is not pure non-existence, because it is not more appropriate to make "above" an expression for the absence of "below" than the reverse. For if we make each of them a non-existence of the other, while each of them in itself is non-existence, then each of them would be a non-existence of non-existence, so each of them would be an existent thing [and if we make each of them an existent attribute], then it would necessarily follow that these places (al-ahiyaz) described by these existent attributes are [existent], because it is impossible for an existent attribute to subsist in pure negation and sheer non-existence. So these proofs and their like are clear and evident proofs for establishing that these places must necessarily be existent.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from an opponent (likely a natural philosopher) that places (al-ahiyaz) must be real, existent entities. The argument begins with the example of "above" and "below." If these were mere negations of each other, then each would be the non-existence of the other. But since each in itself is non-existence, the result would be a non-existence of non-existence, which is an existent thing. Alternatively, if they are considered existent attributes, then the places described by them must be existent, because an existent attribute cannot inhere in pure nothingness. The opponent concludes that such proofs clearly establish that places are existent. The argument relies on a parity principle: there is no reason to privilege one direction as the negation of the other.

Key Terms
حيز (hayyiz)
عدم (adam)
فوقية (fawqiyyah)
تحتية (tahtiyyah)
صفة موجودة (sifah mawjudah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument represents the mutakallim's own position.
Correction: It is the opponent's argument, which will be subsequently refuted in the kalam discussion.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the opponent's argument in this passage?
A) Above and below are pure non-existence.
B) Places must be existent entities.
C) Attributes cannot subsist in negation.
D) The mutakallim's view is correct.

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that spatial directions cannot be pure non-existence, leading to the conclusion that places (ahiyaz) must be real, existent entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of directions like 'above' and 'below', do you automatically assume they are real or just relative ideas? How does the opponent's logical argument challenge you to examine the foundations of what you consider 'real'?
?
This argument from the opponent uses pure logic to prove that places exist. In your faith, do you rely more on logical proofs or on revelation for certainty? Is it possible to have both, and if so, which one holds more weight in your heart?
?
If you were to present a logical proof for a fundamental belief (like the existence of God), would your conviction be shaken if someone pointed out a flaw in your logic? How do you guard your faith against over-reliance on intellectual arguments?

Places as Mental Constructs: The Mutakallim's Proof
The mutakallim answers that places are mental constructs and divides the possibilities of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's argument that places are mental constructs, not externally existent entities, and how they use a dichotomy to prove this.

Core Idea
Places are mental constructs, not external realities — the mutakallimun's key insight.

In this passage, the mutakallimun respond directly to the objection that an infinite regress of places is impossible. They assert that places are not real external entities but mere mental suppositions. To prove this, they offer a powerful argument by dichotomy, showing that if places were real, they would have to be either perceptible or imperceptible — both impossible. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the mutakallimun first make their claim and then provide a proof.
The passage is dense but clear.
2
Identify the two-part proof
The proof is a dichotomy: if places existed, they would be either 'pointed to' (sensible) or not. Find these two divisions in the text.
3
Understand why both are false
The commentary explains that each division leads to impossibility. Reflect on why the mutakallimun consider both options false.
This shows the strength of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب المتكلون فقالوا : هذه الأحياز أمور يفرضها الذهن ، ويقدرها العقل ، ويحكم بكون الأجسام حاصلة فيها [ ونافذة فيها ] وأما في [ الوجود الخارجي ] فلا وجود لها البتة. والذي يدل على أنه لا وجود لها البتة : أنها لو كانت موجودة ، لكانت إما أن يكون وجودها وجوداً مشاراً إليه [ أو غير مشار إليه ] والقسمان باطلان فبطل القول بكونها موجودة.
And the mutakallimun answered, saying: These places are things that the mind supposes, and the intellect estimates, and judges that bodies occur in them [and penetrate them]. But as for [external existence], they have no existence at all. And what indicates that they have no existence at all is that if they were existent, they would be either an existence that is pointed to [or not pointed to], and both divisions are false, so the statement that they are existent is false.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage gives the mutakallim's direct response to the opponent's argument. The mutakallimun assert that places (al-ahiyaz) are purely mental constructs: the mind hypothesizes them, the intellect estimates them, and judges that bodies occur in them or penetrate them. But in external reality, they have no existence whatsoever. To prove this, they argue by dichotomy: if places were existent, their existence would either be something that can be pointed to (perceptible by the senses) or not. Both possibilities are false, as will be detailed in the following lessons. Therefore, the claim that places are existent is false. This response sets up the subsequent refutations.

Key Terms
ذهن (dhihn)
عقل (aql)
وهم (wahm)
وجود خارجي (wujud khariji)
مشار إليه (mushar ilayhi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun deny the reality of bodies or that bodies occupy space.
Correction: They deny only the independent existence of an empty container apart from bodies, not that bodies exist in a relational sense.

Checkpoint
How do the mutakallimun characterize places (al-ahiyaz) in this passage?
A) As real, extended substances.
B) As things that the mind supposes and the intellect estimates.
C) As accidents inhering in bodies.
D) As self-subsistent entities.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun argue that places are purely mental constructs, not externally real, and their existence would lead to impossible consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the places you have filled with your actions—your prayer rug, your desk, your bed—do you realize that these 'places' have no independent existence? What does that mean for the weight you give to physical locations in your spiritual life?

The mutakallimun argued that if places were existent, they would be either sensible or not—both false. In your life, what things do you consider 'real' simply because you can point to them? What about invisible realities like intentions? Have you neglected your inner state for outer appearances?

Refuting Place as Substance or Accident
Refutation that place is a substance or accident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to kalam, place cannot be an existent entity—whether as an independent body or a dependent accident.

Core Idea
Place is not an extramental reality; both substance and accident views lead to absurdities.

Before settling what place truly is, the mutakallim first eliminates wrong possibilities. He reasons: if place exists and can be pointed to, it must be either independent (a substance) or dependent (an accident). In this passage, he systematically refutes both, showing that each leads to impossible consequences such as infinite regress or mutual inherence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical division
The mutakallim begins by dividing every existent pointed to into two categories: independent (istiqlal) or dependent (taba'iyyah). Grasp this key distinction.
This division is exhaustive; there is no third option.
2
First possibility: place as a body
Read the argument that if place is a body, it would need another place, leading to an infinite chain of bodies. Then consider the sub-argument about two bodies: if one occurs in another, either they just touch or interpenetrate—both impossible.
The touch option reduces place to a surface (Aristotle's view), not void space.
3
Second possibility: place as an accident
If place is a dependent existent (accident), it would inhere in a body. But the placed object also inheres in place, causing each to be both a locus and an occupant—an absurd mutual inherence.
An accident cannot be a locus for a substance.
4
Synthesize the refutation
With both possibilities refuted, conclude that place is not an existent pointed to at all—it is a mental consideration, not an external reality.
This denial is only extramental; place as a concept remains useful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون موجوداً مشاراً إليه . لأن كل ما كان موجوداً مشاراً إليه ، فإما أن يكون كذلك بالاستقلال أو بالتبعية . فإن كان موجوداً مشاراً إليه بالاستقلال فذلك هو الجسم ، فحينئذ يكون الشيء المسمى بالحيز جسماً. لكن كل جسم فهو محتاج إلى حيز آخر ، فيلزم افتقار كل جسم إلى جسم آخر ، لا إلى نهاية ، وهو محال . وأيضاً : فهذا الجسم الذي سميناه بالحيز ، إذا حصل فيه جسم آخر ، فهذا الحصول إما أن يكون مفسراً بأن أحد الجسمين يماس الآخر ، أو يكون مفسراً بأنه ينفذ فيه ويسري فيه . فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يصير معنى الحيز والمكان [ والجهة ] هو السطح الحاوي ، وهذا رجوع إلى قول [ أرسطاطاليس ] واعتراف بأن المكان ليس عبارة عن الفضاء والخلاء . وإن كان الثاني : فحينئذ يلزم نفوذ أحد الجسمين في الآخر ، لكن القول بتداخل الأجسام باطل ، فكان هذا القول باطلاً. وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال : هذا الحيز موجود ، مشار إليه بتبعية الغير. فنقول : لا معنى للعرض إلا ذلك . فلو كان المكان عبارة عنه ، لزم كون المكان عرضاً حالاً في الجسم. لكن المتمكن حال في المكان ، فيلزم أن يكون ذلك الجوهر وذلك العرض ، يكون كل واحد منهما حالاً في الآخر [ ومحلاً له ] وذلك محال.
As for our saying: it is not permissible that it be an existent pointed to. Because everything that is existent pointed to is either so independently or dependently. If it is existent pointed to independently, then that is the body, so then the thing called place would be a body. But every body is in need of another place, so it would necessitate every body needing another body without end, which is impossible. Also, this body that we called place, if another body occurs in it, then this occurrence is either interpreted as one of the two bodies touching the other, or interpreted as it penetrating and pervading it. If the first, then the meaning of place, location [and direction] becomes the containing surface, and this is a return to the statement of [Aristotle] and an admission that place is not an expression for space and void. If the second, then it would necessitate the penetration of one body into the other, but the statement of interpenetration of bodies is false, so this statement is false. As for the second division, which is that it be said: this place is existent, pointed to by dependence on another. We say: there is no meaning of accident except that. So if place were an expression for it, it would necessitate that place be an accident inhering in the body. But the placed thing (al-mutamakkin) is present in the place, so it would necessitate that the substance and the accident each be present in the other [and a locus for it], and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage details the refutation of two possible ways a place could be existent and perceptible. The mutakallim first clarifies the division: any existent that can be pointed to is either independent (a substance) or dependent (an accident).

First possibility: Place as an independent existent. This would make place a body. But every body requires another place (since bodies are located), leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Furthermore, if a body (place) contains another body, either they touch or interpenetrate. If they touch, then place reduces to the containing surface (Aristotle's view), which admits place is not void space. If they interpenetrate, that is impossible (bodies cannot occupy the same space). So the first possibility fails.

Second possibility: Place as a dependent existent, i.e., an accident. An accident by definition inheres in a body. But then place would be an accident inhering in a body, while the placed body (the thing located) inheres in the place. This would result in each being both a locus and an occupant for the other, which is absurd. Thus, both possibilities are false, so place cannot be an existent pointed to.

Key Terms
استقلال (istiqlal)
تبعية (taba'iyyah)
جسم (jism)
عرض (arad)
حلول (hulul)
تمكن (tamakkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument denies place any conceptual usefulness.
Correction: The argument only denies place extramental existence; place as a mental concept or relational notion remains valid.

Checkpoint
Why does the mutakallim reject the possibility that place is an independent body?
A) Because a body cannot be pointed to.
B) Because it would lead to an infinite regress of bodies needing places.
C) Because bodies cannot touch each other.
D) Because place cannot be a surface.

Key Takeaway
Place is not an existent entity; both substance and accident views are rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's existence being without a place (lā makān), does this argument help you detach from physical images you might unconsciously hold? What specific image do you need to let go of?
?
The argument uses logical impossibility to negate a common-sense notion of place. In what area of your life do you rely on a concept that, if examined logically, would collapse? Are you willing to pursue that examination today?
?
If the infinite regress of places is impossible, what does that tell you about your own need for a final grounding? Can you sincerely say that you have placed your ultimate trust in something that does not itself require a place?

Refutation of Imperceptible Place and Final Conclusion
The mutakallim refutes the possibility that place is imperceptible and concludes it is only a mental estimation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallim concludes that place is not an independent existent but a mental estimation based on perceived bodily interactions.

Core Idea
Place is not a container; it is a mental estimation of bodily positions.

The mutakallim has already eliminated two possibilities for the nature of place: that it is a perceptible independent entity or a perceptible dependent entity. Now he tackles the final option: that place exists but is imperceptible. Through a simple empirical observation, he shows this too is untenable, leading to a sweeping conclusion about the nature of place.

Guided Path
1
Encounter the third possibility
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the mutakallim is addressing the claim that place exists but cannot be sensed.
2
Follow the refutation
Identify the key empirical observation: we point to a body moving from one place to another. How does this refute imperceptibility?
The act of pointing implies perceptibility of the previous and new locations.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Understand that the mutakallim concludes place is only a mental estimation (wahm). What does this mean for our everyday concept of space?
It means that only bodies are real; their positions are relative and mentally constructed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : المكان موجود غير مشار إليه بحسب الحس. فهذا أيضاً باطل. لأنا نشير بالحس إلى أن هذا الجسم انتقل من هذا الحيز إلى الحيز الثاني. وذلك يقتضي كون الحيز المنتقل عنه، والحيز المنتقل إليه أمراً مشاراً إليه بالحس، وذلك يقتضي أن يكون القول بأن المكان موجود غير مشار إليه بحسب الحس باطلاً. فيثبت بما ذكرنا: أن هذا المكان لو كان موجوداً، لكان إما أن يكون موجوداً مشاراً إليه بحسب الحس، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك. وثبت فساد كل واحد من القسمين، فيلزم القطع بأن المكان [ والحيز ] ليس شيئاً موجوداً في نفسه، بل أمر يفرضه ويقدره الوهم، وأن المشار إليه بحسب الحس ليس إلا الجسم. وإذا كان يماس جسماً، ثم انتقل عنه، وصار يماس جسماً آخر، فهذا هو المشار إليه بحسب الحس جسم وليس ههنا شيء آخر سواه.
As for the third division, which is that it be said: place is existent but not pointed to by sense. This too is false. Because we point by sense to the fact that this body moved from this place to the second place. And that requires that the place moved from and the place moved to be something pointed to by sense, and that requires that the statement that place is existent but not pointed to by sense be false. So it is established by what we have mentioned that if this place were existent, it would be either existent pointed to by sense or not so. And the corruption of each of the two divisions has been established, so it necessarily follows that place [and space] is not something existent in itself, but rather something that estimation (al-wahm) supposes and estimates, and that what is pointed to by sense is only the body. And when it touches a body, then moves away from it and comes to touch another body, this is what is pointed to by sense: body, and there is nothing else here besides it.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage deals with the third possibility: that place exists but is imperceptible (not pointed to by the senses). The mutakallim refutes this by appealing to empirical observation: we can see a body moving from one place to another. That act of pointing to where it was and where it went implies that the previous and new locations are perceptible. If they were imperceptible, we could not sensibly distinguish them. Therefore, the claim that place is imperceptible is false.

Having established that all three possibilities (existent and perceptible independently, existent and perceptible dependently, existent and imperceptible) are impossible, the conclusion follows decisively: place (al-makan) and space (al-hayyiz) are not things that exist in themselves. Rather, they are mental constructs posited by estimation (al-wahm). What is actually pointed to by the senses is only the body itself. When a body touches another body and then moves to touch a different body, that sequence of contact is all that is sensibly perceived; there is no additional entity called "place" between them.

Key Terms
حس (hiss)
وهم (wahm)
مشار إليه بحسب الحس (mushar ilayhi bi-hasab al-hiss)
المكان (al-makan)
الحيز (al-hayyiz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallim denies that objects occupy any positions at all.
Correction: The mutakallim does not deny that objects occupy positions; they deny that there is a separate container or void apart from the objects themselves. Positions are relational and mentally constructed.

Checkpoint
What empirical observation does the mutakallim use to refute the claim that place is imperceptible?
A) We can feel the texture of place.
B) We can point to the body moving from one place to another.
C) Place is always the same as the body's surface.
D) Place can be imagined even if not perceived.

Key Takeaway
Place is not an independent entity; it is a mental estimation based on the observed positions of bodies in contact and motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely thought of 'place' as a real container. After reading this lesson, are you willing to accept that place is only a mental estimation, or do you still feel a resistance? What would it mean for your daily life if you truly internalized that the positions of objects are all that exist, with no empty space between them?
?
The mutakallim uses sense perception to prove that place cannot be imperceptible. How much do you trust your senses when it comes to matters of faith? Is there a danger in relying too heavily on what is perceptible? What does this lesson teach you about the limitations of your senses?
?
If place is not a real thing, then what about other concepts like time or causality? Could they also be mental estimations? How does that affect your view of Allah's creation and your own existence? Do you find this unsettling or liberating?

The Mutakallimun's Refutation of Space as an Independent Entity
A proof by division refuting the independent existence of space.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's proof by division that space (al-makan) does not exist as an independent reality but is merely a mental construct.

Core Idea
Space is not a real thing; it is an imagined framework we project onto bodies.

The mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) argued vigorously against the existence of space as an independent entity. Using a method called taqsim (proof by division), they considered all possible ways space could exist and showed each leads to absurdity. This passage presents their decisive argument, concluding that what we perceive as 'space' is only the bodies themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once slowly, then the translation. Notice the logical flow: the author divides the possibilities and refutes each.
The passage uses technical terms like al-hayyiz, al-makan, al-'arad, al-jawhar.
2
Identify the three divisions
The author considers three ways space could exist. Find each one and see how it is refuted.
First division: space as surface (Aristotle's view) or leading to interpenetration. Second: space as an accident. Third: space as imperceptible.
3
Understand the refutation of each division
For each division, note the logical consequence that is impossible. For example, the second leads to substance and accident being in each other.
4
Grasp the conclusion
After refuting the two main possibilities (perceptible or imperceptible), the author concludes space is a mental estimation by wahm (imagination).
The final sentence summarizes: what is pointed to by sense is only the body itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يصير معنى الحيز والمكان [والجهة] هو السطح الحاوي، وهذا رجوع إلى قول [أرسطاطاليس] واعتراف بأن المكان ليس عبارة عن الفضاء والخلاء. وإن كان الثاني: فحينئذ يلزم نفوذ أحد الجسمين في الآخر، لكن القول بتداخل الأجسام باطل، فكان هذا القول باطلاً. وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال: هذا الحيز موجود، مشار إليه بتبعية الغير. فنقول: لا معنى للعرض إلا ذلك. فلو كان المكان عبارة عنه، لزم كون المكان عرضاً حالاً في الجسم. لكن المتمكن حال في المكان، فيلزم أن يكون ذلك الجوهر وذلك العرض، يكون كل واحد منهما حالاً في الآخر [ومحلاً له] وذلك محال. وأما القسم الثالث: وهو أن يقال: المكان موجود غير مشار إليه بحسب الحس. فهذا أيضاً باطل. لأنا نشير بالحس إلى أن هذا الجسم انتقل من هذا الحيز إلى الحيز [الثاني]. وذلك يقتضي كون الحيز المنتقل عنه، والحيز المنتقل إليه أمراً مشاراً إليه بالحس، وذلك يقتضي أن يكون القول بأن المكان موجود، غير مشار إليه بحسب الحس باطلاً. فيثبت بما ذكرنا: أن هذا المكان لو كان موجوداً، لكان إما أن يكون موجوداً مشاراً إليه بحسب الحس، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك. وثبت فساد كل واحد من القسمين، فيلزم القطع بأن المكان [والحيز] ليس شيئاً موجوداً في نفسه، بل أمر يفرضه ويقدره الوهم، وأن المشار إليه بحسب الحس ليس إلا الجسم. وإذا كان يماس جسماً، ثم انتقل عنه، وصار يماس جسماً آخر، فهذا هو المشار إليه بحسب الحس، وليس ههنا شيء آخر سواه.
If it is the first, then the meaning of spatial extension (al-hayyiz) and place (al-makan) [and direction] becomes the encompassing surface, and this is a reversion to the statement of [Aristotle] and an admission that place is not expressed by void (al-fada') and empty space (al-khala'). If it is the second, then the penetration of one body into the other becomes necessary, but the statement of the interpenetration of bodies is invalid, so this statement is invalid. As for the second division, that it is said: this spatial extension exists, pointed to by dependence on another. We say: there is no meaning for accident (al-'arad) except that. So if place were expressed by it, it would necessitate place being an accident occurring in the body. But the occupant (al-mutamakkin) occurs in place, so it would necessitate that the substance (al-jawhar) and the accident, each one of them, occurs in the other [and is a locus for it], and that is impossible. As for the third division: that it is said: place exists, not pointed to by sense. This also is invalid, because we point by sense to the fact that this body has moved from this spatial extension to the [second] spatial extension. That necessitates that the spatial extension moved from and the spatial extension moved to are things pointed to by sense, and that necessitates that the statement that place exists, not pointed to by sense, is invalid. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that if this place existed, it would either be an existent pointed to by sense, or not be so. And the corruption of each of the two divisions is established, so cutting is necessitated that place [and spatial extension] are not a thing existing in themselves, but rather a matter that imagination (al-wahm) supposes and estimates, and that what is pointed to by sense is nothing but the body. And when it contacts a body, then moves away from it and comes to contact another body, this is what is pointed to by sense, and there is nothing other than it here.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a proof by division (taqsim) that space (al-makan) does not exist as an independent entity. The author begins by considering two possibilities for the meaning of space: either it is a surface that contains the body (the view of Aristotle) or it involves penetration of one body into another. The first leads back to Aristotle's view, implying space is not void. The second leads to the absurdity of interpenetration of bodies. Then the author considers a second division: space is an existent but perceived only through its relation to something else. This would make space an accident (arad), but then the substance (jawhar) and accident would each be located in the other, which is impossible. Third, if space is existent but not perceptible, that is also false because we perceive bodies moving from one space to another, so those spaces must be perceptible. Therefore, the only possibility is that space is not a real existent but a mental construct. The conclusion is that what is perceived is only the body itself.

Key Terms
المكان (al-makan)
الحيز (al-hayyiz)
الخلاء (al-khala')
العرض (al-'arad)
الجوهر (al-jawhar)
المتمكن (al-mutamakkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the mutakallimun deny any reality of space, even as a mental concept.
Correction: They only deny space as an independent entity; they affirm it as a mental estimation (farḍ al-wahm).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot space be existent but imperceptible?
A) Because if imperceptible, it could not be a container.
B) Because we perceive bodies moving from one space to another, so spaces must be perceptible.
C) Because imperceptible things cannot exist.
D) Because space is a mental construct.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun prove that space is not an independently existing thing; it is merely a mental estimation (farḍ al-wahm) of the relationships between bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You move through the world assuming that the space around you is a real container for objects and events. But this lesson argues that space is merely a mental estimation. How does this challenge your everyday perception of reality? Are you willing to question the basic framework through which you experience the world? What would change if you truly believed that only bodies exist and space is a construct of your mind?
?
The proof refutes the notion that space could be an accident because it would require mutual occupancy between substance and accident. Have you ever tried to pinpoint exactly what 'space' is when you look at an empty room? This lesson calls that presumption into question. Can you identify a specific moment today when you assumed space was a real thing, and how might you remind yourself that it is only a mental estimate?
?
The mutakallimun conclude that what we point to is only the body itself and its relationships. Consider your attachment to physical locations—your home, your prayer space, your bed. If these 'places' are not real entities but merely your estimation of body relations, does that mean you can never truly 'be' in a space? How does this reshape your understanding of presence in the world? What does this imply about your relationship with the material world as a believer?
