The Philosophers' Argument that Space Must Exist
The philosophers argue that space must exist as a real container.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' rational argument that spatial extensions must be real existent containers, not mere mental constructs.

Core Idea
Space must be real because motion and rest are impossible without real containers.

The philosophers (al-hukama') respond to the mutakallimun's claim that spaces are only mental constructs. They argue that this claim contradicts the intuitive judgment of the intellect and the reality of motion and rest. The passage presents a powerful rational argument that spaces must exist as external realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two possibilities
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the philosophers start by rejecting the mutakallimun's view and then present a disjunction: either the judgment that bodies are in spaces corresponds to external reality or it does not.
The disjunction is the key structure of the argument.
2
Understand the first branch: if the judgment corresponds
If the judgment corresponds to external reality, then bodies are actually in spaces. But for space to be a container, it must itself exist, because pure non-existence cannot be a container or have attributes like size and position.
The philosophers argue that attributes like small/large, near/far cannot apply to non-existence.
3
Understand the second branch: if the judgment does not correspond
If the judgment does not correspond, then it is false, like imagining a wall is a ruby. However, motion and rest are real attributes of bodies. Motion means changing from one space to another, rest means staying in one space. This implies spaces are real.
The philosophers use the reality of motion and rest to prove the real existence of spaces.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The philosophers conclude that the mutakallimun's view—that empty spatial extensions are only mental constructs—is invalid. Consider how this argument relies on basic intuitions about motion and rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الحكماء: أما قولكم: إن هذه الأحياز أمور يفرضها العقل ويقدرها الوهم ويحكم بكون الأجسام حاصلة فيها. مع أنه لا وجود لها في نفس الأمر. فهذا الكلام مدفوع في بديهة العقل. وذلك لأن حكم الذهن والعقل بأن هذه الأجسام حاصلة في هذه الأحياز. إما أن يكون حكماً مطابقاً للوجود الخارجي، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك. فإن كان الأول كان الجسم في نفسه حاصلاً في الحيز، إلا أن كون الحيز ظرفاً للجسم يعتمد كون الحيز في نفسه أمراً موجوداً. لأن العدم المحض يمتنع كونه ظرفاً للجسم، ويمتنع كونه مشاراً إليه بحسب الحس، ويمتنع وصفه بالصغر والكبير، والطول والقصر، والقرب والبعد، والاتصال والانفصال. وإذا كان كذلك، وجب أن يكون الحيز في نفسه أمراً موجوداً. وأما إن كان هذا الحكم الذهني غير مطابق للأمر الخارجي في نفسه، فحينئذ يكون هذا الحكم حكماً كاذباً وفرضاً باطلاً، وجارياً مجرى ما إذا فرضنا أن هذا الجدار ياقوت، مع أنه ليس في نفسه فذلك. ومعلوم أن هذا باطل. لأن الجسم في نفسه موصوف بأنه متحرك، وبأنه ساكن. ولا معنى لكونه متحركاً إلا أنه انتقل من حيز إلى حيز، ومن جهة إلى جهة. ولا معنى لكونه ساكناً إلا أنه استقر في حيز واحد زماناً طويلاً. فيثبت: أن قول المتكلمين: إن هذه الأحياز الفارغة أمور لا حصول لها إلا بحسب الوهم والفرض والخيال: كلام باطل.
The philosophers (al-hukama') said: As for your statement that these spatial extensions are things that the intellect supposes and the imagination estimates and judges that bodies occur in them, while they have no existence in actual fact—this statement is refuted by the intuitive faculty of the intellect. That is because the judgment of the mind and intellect that these bodies occur in these spatial extensions: either it is a judgment conforming to external existence, or it is not. If it is the first, then the body itself occurs in the spatial extension, except that the spatial extension being a container (zarf) for the body depends on the spatial extension itself being an existent thing. For pure non-existence (al-'adam al-mahd) cannot be a container for the body, nor can it be pointed to by sense, nor can it be described as small or large, long or short, near or far, connected or disconnected. And if that is so, then the spatial extension must itself be an existent thing. And if this mental judgment does not conform to the external thing itself, then this judgment is a false judgment and an invalid supposition, running in the course of what if we suppose that this wall is a ruby, while in itself it is not that. It is known that this is invalid. For the body in itself is described as moving and as at rest. And there is no meaning for its being moving except that it transfers from one spatial extension to another, and from one direction to another. And there is no meaning for its being at rest except that it settles in one spatial extension for a long time. So it is established: that the statement of the mutakallimun that these empty spatial extensions are things that have no existence except according to imagination, supposition, and fancy—is an invalid statement.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is the philosophers' (al-hukama') response to the mutakallimun. They argue that the statement that spaces are merely mental constructs is refuted by the intuition of the intellect. The judgment that bodies are in spaces must either correspond to external reality or not. If it corresponds, then space must exist as a container, because pure non-existence cannot be a container, nor can it be perceived, nor described with attributes. If it does not correspond, then the judgment is false, like imagining a wall to be ruby. But we truly attribute motion and rest to bodies: motion is movement from one space to another, rest is remaining in one space for a time. Therefore, the mutakallimun's view that spaces are only mental is false.

Key Terms
الحكماء (al-hukama')
البديهة (al-badihah)
العدم المحض (al-'adam al-mahd)
الظرف (al-zarf)
المطابقة (al-mutabaqah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers claim that space is a substance or an independent entity.
Correction: The philosophers only argue that space must exist as a container (zarf) for bodies, not that it is a substance. They do not specify its ontological category.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' argument against the claim that spaces are mental constructs?
A) If spaces were mental, motion and rest would be meaningless because they are defined by transfer between or remaining in real spaces.
B) The intellect perceives spaces directly as physical realities.
C) The mutakallimun's view contradicts Aristotle's physics.
D) Space must be a substance like a body.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers prove that spatial extensions must be real existent containers because motion and rest are real and defined by change or stability in space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers prove that space must be real because without it, motion and rest have no meaning. Now consider your own spiritual journey: you move from sin to repentance, from heedlessness to remembrance. If the 'container' of your heart's intentions is imaginary, then what is the reality of your striving? Are you resting on empty assumptions about your own faith?
?
Pure non-existence cannot be a container, the philosophers say. Yet how often do you try to contain your deepest anxieties or your love for Allah in vague daydreams rather than concrete actions? What would it mean for you to treat your spiritual life as truly existing—with real spaces to move through and real deeds to fill them?
?
The mutakallimun claimed spaces are only mental constructs, but the philosophers showed that this contradicts the reality of motion. Think of an area in your life where you are convinced something is 'just in your head'—perhaps a doubt about God's mercy or a hope for improvement. Does dismissing its reality free you from responsibility, or does it keep you from acting on the truth? What would it cost you to treat that thing as truly existent and respond accordingly?

The Philosophers' Rebuttal of the Division Argument on Space
The philosophers respond to the division argument for space's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' defense of space as an independent, perceptible dimension and their critique of the mutakallimun's division argument.

Core Idea
Space is an independent dimension, and its penetration by bodies is possible.

The mutakallimun argued that if space existed, it must be either perceptible or imperceptible, then concluded it is imperceptible and thus impossible. The philosophers respond directly, asserting that space is indeed perceptible and that it is a self-subsistent entity. They further reject the mutakallimun's claim that penetration of dimensions is impossible as a mere assertion. This passage sets the stage for a detailed discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation to understand the philosophers' argument.
Notice the phrase 'محض الدعوى' (mere claim) as a key critique.
2
Identify the philosophers' main claims
Note that the philosophers make two key claims: space is perceptible, and it is an independent dimension. Also, they dismiss the impossibility of dimensional penetration as an unproven claim.
3
Understand the polemical context
Realize that this is a rebuttal: the philosophers are responding to the mutakallimun's division. The phrase 'mere claim' is a powerful dismissive critique.
The philosophers promise a detailed discussion later, indicating this is an introductory response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التقسيم الذي ذكروه من أنه لو كان موجوداً، لكان إما أن يكون موجوداً مشاراً إليه بحسب الحس، أو لا يكون كذلك. فنقول: إنه عندنا موجود مشار إليه بحسب الحس. وإنه بعد قائم بنفسه، مستقل بذاته والأجسام إذا حصلت فيها نفدت أبعاد المتمكن في هذه الأبعاد المسماة بأنها الأمكنة والأحياز. فقولهم: إن نفود البعد في البعد محال. فهذا محض الدعوى. وسنتكلم في هذه المسألة بالاستقصاء إن شاء الله تعالى.
And as for the division which they mentioned, that if it existed, it would either be an existent pointed to by sense, or not be so. We say: It is, according to us, an existent pointed to by sense. And moreover it is self-subsistent (qa'im bi-nafsihi), independent in its essence, and when bodies occur in them, the dimensions (ab'ad) of the occupant penetrate into these dimensions, which are called places (amkinah) and spatial extensions (ahyahiz). So their statement that the penetration of a dimension into a dimension is impossible—this is a mere claim. And we will speak about this matter in detail, God willing.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is the philosophers' direct response to the mutakallimun's earlier division (that if space existed, it would be either perceptible or not). The philosophers assert that space is perceptible and that it is an independent, self-subsistent entity. When bodies are located in spaces, the dimensions of the body penetrate into the dimensions of the space (which they call places and spaces). So their claim that penetration of one dimension into another is impossible is mere assertion, not proof. They promise to discuss the matter in detail later.

Key Terms
النفوذ (al-nufudh)
البعد (al-bu'd)
القائم بنفسه (al-qa'im bi-nafsihi)
المستقل (al-mustaqill)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the philosophers claim space is a body.
Correction: The philosophers do not claim that space is a body; they claim it is an independent dimension (بعد قائم بنفسه).

Checkpoint
How do the philosophers respond to the claim that penetration of dimensions is impossible?
A) They accept it as true.
B) They say it is a mere assertion without proof.
C) They prove it is possible by example.
D) They deny that penetration occurs.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers assert that space is a perceptible, independent dimension and reject the mutakallimun's claim of impossible penetration as unsubstantiated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Dilemma of Contact and Interpenetration
Author presents a dilemma from the Aristotelian definition of contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Aristotelian definition of contact leads to a logical dilemma that supports the possibility of interpenetration.

Core Idea
Contact defined by shared boundaries allows interpenetration through logical necessity.

This lesson presents a powerful objection against the Peripatetic definition of contact. The author shows that the definition, if analyzed rigorously, leads to a series of logical dilemmas. By exploring these dilemmas, we see that the Aristotelian framework cannot prevent the possibility of interpenetration—a conclusion that challenges their core physics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of contact
Read the passage and locate the definition from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa: two bodies in contact are those whose extremities are together in position (sensible indication).
This definition is the starting point of the objection.
2
Work through the first horn of the dilemma
Assume a surface touches a surface. Their boundaries (lines) are together. If the lines do not remain during contact, a single shared line forms, which is union (ittisal), not contact. This contradicts the assumption of contact.
The definition requires that the extremities remain, so this horn is eliminated.
3
Work through the second horn
If the boundaries remain, either one penetrates the other completely or not. If no penetration, they are separate, and indication to each differs, contradicting the definition. If one completely penetrates the other, then consider the total magnitude.
The key sub-case is complete penetration.
4
Analyze the sub-case of complete penetration
If one line completely penetrates the other, the total magnitude either increases or stays the same. If it increases, penetration is not complete; if it stays the same, then two extents interpenetrate without increase. This is conceivable.
This logical possibility opens the door to interpenetration.
5
Connect to the broader issue
The author argues that if such interpenetration is conceivable for lines, then it is also conceivable for the dimension of a body and the dimension of place, thus supporting the possibility of interpenetration in physics.
This conclusion challenges Aristotelian physics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : وهو أن مثل هذا الإشكال لازم على أصحاب أرسطاطاليس ، فإنهم قالوا : « المتماسان [ هما اللذان ] طرفاهما معاً في الوضع ، أي في الإشارة الحسية. قال الشيخ في الشفاء : و المتماسان هما اللذان تقع الإشارة الحسية على طرفيهما معاً ، فأقول : لنفرض سطحاً ماس سطحاً ، فههنا يلزم أن يكون طرفاهما معاً ، يعني : الخطان اللذان هما نهايتهما يكونان معاً في الوضع . فنقول : هذان الخطان إما أن يكونا باقيين حال حصول هذه المماسة أو لا يكونان باقيين . فإن لم يبقيا بل حدث خط واحد يكون هو بعينه مشتركاً فيه بين ذينك السطحين . فهذا يكون اتصالاً لا مماسة . وقد فرضنا أن الحاصل هو المماسة لا الاتصال . وأيضاً فقول الشيخ : ( المتماسان هما اللذان طرفاهما معاً في الوضع ، يقتضي أن يكون طرفاهما باقيين [ حال التماس ] وذلك يمنع من القول بأنهما عدماً وحصل طرف واحد مشترك فيه بين السطحين وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : الطرفان باقيان . فإما أن يقال : نفد أحدهما بالكلية في الآخر أو لم ينفد . فإن لم ينفد أحدهما في الآخر كان أحدهما مبايناً عن الآخر . فيلزم أن لا تكون الإشارة إلى أحدهما ، عين الإشارة إلى الآخر ، مع أنا قد فرضناهما متماسين. هذا خلف . وأما إن قلنا : إن أحد هذين الخطين قد نفد بكليته في كلية الخط الآخر. فإما أن يكون مقدار هذا المجموع أزيد من مقدار الواحد أو لا يكون . فإن كان الأول فحينئذ لا تكون كلية أحد هذين الخطين نافدة في كلية الخط الآخر ، وقد فرضنا الأمر كذلك وهذا خلف . وإن كان الثاني فقد حصل ههنا مقداران ونفد أحدهما في كلية الآخر . مع أنه لم يصر مقدار المجموع أزيد من مقدار الواحد ، وإذا عقلنا ذلك في هذه الصورة ، فلم لا يجوز مثله في بعد المتمكن وبعد المكان ؟ وهذا سؤال قوي على
The second question: That this same problem is binding upon the followers of Aristotle, for they said: 'Two bodies in contact are those whose two extremities are together in position, i.e., in sensible indication.' The Sheikh said in al-Shifa: 'Two bodies in contact are those for which sensible indication falls upon both their extremities together.' So I say: Let us assume a surface touching a surface. Here it is necessary that their two extremities be together, meaning the two lines which are their boundaries are together in position. We say: These two lines either remain at the time of this contact or they do not remain. If they do not remain, but a single line occurs which is itself shared between those two surfaces, then this would be union (ittisal), not contact. And we have assumed that what occurs is contact, not union. Also, the saying of the Sheikh: 'Two bodies in contact are those whose two extremities are together in position' requires that their two extremities remain during contact, and that prevents saying that they are annihilated and a single shared boundary occurs between the two surfaces. As for the second division: that it is said the two extremities remain. Then either it is said that one of them completely penetrates into the other or it does not penetrate. If one of them does not penetrate into the other, then one of them is separate from the other. Then it would follow that the indication to one of them is not the same as the indication to the other, while we have assumed them to be in contact. This is a contradiction. And if we say that one of these two lines has completely penetrated into the totality of the other line, then either the magnitude of this total is greater than the magnitude of one or it is not. If it is the first, then the totality of one of these two lines is not penetrating into the totality of the other line, and we have assumed that to be the case. This is a contradiction. And if it is the second, then there have occurred here two extents and one has penetrated into the totality of the other, while the magnitude of the total has not become greater than the magnitude of one. And if we understand that in this case, then why should its like not be permissible in the case of the dimension of the located body and the dimension of place? And this is a strong objection against

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection: the same logical problem about interpenetration applies to the Aristotelian definition of contact (تماس/مماسة). The author cites the definition from Ibn Sina's (the Sheikh) al-Shifa: 'Two bodies in contact are those whose extremities are together in position (sensible indication).' Assuming a surface touching a surface, their boundaries (two lines) are together. The author then poses a dilemma: either these lines remain during contact or they do not. If they do not remain, a single line forms that is shared, which is union (اتصال), not contact. This contradicts the assumption of contact. Also, the definition requires that the extremities remain. So the lines must remain. Then if they remain, either one penetrates (ينفد) the other completely or not. If not, they are separate, so the indication to each is different, contradicting the definition's requirement that indication is to both together. If one completely penetrates the other, then either the total magnitude increases or not. If it increases, then the penetrating line does not fully penetrate, contradicting the assumption. If it does not increase, then we have two extents with full penetration without increase in magnitude. The author concludes that if this is conceivable for lines, then it is also conceivable for the dimension of a body (بعد المتمكن) and the dimension of place (بعد المكان), thus supporting the possibility of interpenetration.

اتصال
مماسة
خط
سطح
إشارة حسية
وضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contact always involves two distinct boundaries that remain separate.
Correction: The Aristotelian definition leads to a dilemma where boundaries either merge (union) or one penetrates the other, so distinct boundaries are not guaranteed.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the analogy between contact of surfaces and interpenetration of body and space?
A) It is invalid
B) It shows that interpenetration is impossible
C) It shows that interpenetration is possible if total magnitude does not increase
D) It proves the existence of a third dimension

Key Takeaway
The Aristotelian definition of contact leads to a logical dilemma that makes interpenetration conceivable, challenging their physical theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about a simple concept, like 'contact', without realizing it hides a logical contradiction? How does this dilemma expose the limits of relying on common sense? Will you commit to questioning everyday assumptions in your faith—like what it means for Allah to be 'with' you—using the same rigorous logic?
?
If even the definition of physical contact leads to a paradox that permits interpenetration, how much more careful must you be when conceptualizing attributes of Allah like His 'istiwa' or 'nearness'? Are you willing to let go of anthropomorphic notions, even if they feel natural, in favor of what the revealed text and reason demand? What concrete step will you take this week to refine your understanding of a divine attribute?

The Definition of Contact and Its First Implication
Definition of contact and the first logical case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Aristotelian definition of contact and why it cannot be reduced to union.

Core Idea
Contact requires two distinct boundaries; union merges them into one.

The passage opens with the standard definition of contact from Aristotle and Ibn Sina. It then sets up a thought experiment involving two surfaces touching. The key question is what happens to their boundaries: do they remain or vanish? This lesson explores the first possibility where boundaries vanish, leading to a confusion between contact and union.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of contact
Read the Arabic definition from Aristotle and Ibn Sina. Notice the phrase 'their two ends are together in position'. Reflect on what 'together' means for sensible indication.
2
Consider the thought experiment
Imagine a surface touching another. The boundaries (lines) must be together. The author considers two possibilities: boundaries remain or not. Focus on the first case where boundaries do not remain.
Think: If boundaries vanish, what appears in their place?
3
Contrast contact and union
If the boundaries vanish, a single shared line appears. This would be union (ittiṣāl), not contact. Also, the definition implies that boundaries must remain for contact to occur.
This shows why contact and union are distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أرسطاطاليس، فإنهم قالوا: "المتماسان هما اللذان طرفاهما معاً في الوضع، أي في الإشارة الحسية." قال الشيخ في الشفاء: "والمتماسان هما اللذان تقع الإشارة الحسية على طرفيهما معاً." فأقول: لنفرض سطحاً ماس سطحاً، فههنا يلزم أن يكون طرفاهما معاً، يعني: الخطان اللذان هما نهايتهما يكونان معاً في الوضع. فنقول: هذان الخطان إما أن يكونا باقيين حال حصول هذه المماسة أو لا يكونان باقيين. فإن لم يبقيا بل حدث خط واحد يكون هو بعينه مشتركاً فيه بين ذينك السطحين، فهذا يكون اتصالاً لا مماسة. وقد فرضنا أن الحاصل هو المماسة لا الاتصال. وأيضاً فقول الشيخ: "المتماسان هما اللذان طرفاهما معاً في الوضع"، يقتضي أن يكون طرفاهما باقيين حال التماس، وذلك يمنع من القول بأنهما عدماً وحصل طرف واحد مشترك فيه بين السطحين. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: الطرفان باقيان. فإما أن يقال: نفد أحدهما بالكلية في الآخر أو لم ينفد. فإن لم ينفد أحدهما في الآخر كان أحدهما مبايناً عن الآخر، فيلزم أن لا تكون الإشارة إلى أحدهما عين الإشارة إلى الآخر، مع أنا قد فرضناهما متماسين، هذا خلف. وأما إن قلنا: إن أحد هذين الخطين قد نفد بكليته في كلية الخط الآخر، فإما أن يكون مقدار هذا المجموع أزيد من مقدار الواحد أو لا يكون. فإن كان الأول فحينئذ لا تكون كلية أحد هذين الخطين نافدة في كلية الخط الآخر، وقد فرضنا الأمر كذلك، وهذا خلف. وإن كان الثاني فقد حصل ههنا مقداران ونفد أحدهما في كلية الآخر، مع أنه لم يصر مقدار المجموع أزيد من مقدار الواحد، وإذا عقلنا ذلك في هذه الصورة، فلم لا يجوز مثله في بعد المتمكن وبعد المكان؟ وهذا سؤال قوي على قانون قولهم. ولهم عنه عذر سيأتي ذكره بعد ذلك مع الجواب، إن شاء الله تعالى.
Aristotle, for they said: "The two things in contact are those whose two ends are together in position, i.e., in sensible indication." The Shaykh said in al-Shifa: "And the two things in contact are those upon whose two ends the sensible indication falls together." So I say: let us suppose a surface touching a surface. Here it is necessary that their two ends be together, meaning: the two lines that are their boundaries are together in position. So we say: these two lines either remain during the occurrence of this contact or they do not remain. If they do not remain but a single line occurs that is itself shared between those two surfaces, then this would be a union (ittiṣāl), not a contact (tamās). And we have supposed that what occurs is contact not union. Also, the Shaykh's statement: "The two things in contact are those whose two ends are together in position" necessitates that their two ends remain during contact, and that prevents saying that they are annihilated and a single end shared between the two surfaces occurs. As for the second division: that it is said: the two ends remain. Then it is either said: one of them completely vanishes (nafada) in the other or it does not vanish. For if one of them does not vanish in the other, then one of them is separate from the other, and it follows that the indication of one of them is not identical to the indication of the other, although we have supposed them to be in contact. This is a contradiction. And if we say that one of these two lines has completely vanished in the entirety of the other line, then either the quantity of this sum is greater than the quantity of the one or not. If it is the first, then the entirety of one of these two lines is not penetrating (nāfiḍa) in the entirety of the other line, and we have supposed it to be so, and this is a contradiction. And if it is the second, then there have occurred here two quantities and one has vanished in the entirety of the other, while the quantity of the sum has not become greater than the quantity of the one. And if we conceive that in this form, then why is its like not permissible in the dimension of the located (bu'd al-mutamakkin) and the dimension of place (bu'd al-makān)? And this is a strong objection to the rule of their statement. And they have an excuse for it that will be mentioned later with the answer, God willing.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a logical analysis of the definition of contact (tamās). It begins by quoting Aristotle and Ibn Sina's definition: two things in contact have their ends together in position, i.e., they are perceived together by the senses. The author then sets up a thought experiment: suppose one surface touches another. The boundaries (lines) must be together. He then considers two possibilities: either the boundaries remain during contact, or they do not. If they do not remain, a single line shared between both surfaces appears, which would be union (ittiṣāl), not contact. Since we assumed contact, this is rejected. Moreover, the definition requires the ends to remain. The second possibility: the ends remain. Now a further sub-division: either one boundary completely vanishes (nafada) in the other or not. If it does not vanish, the ends remain separate, so the sensible indication of each is distinct, contradicting their being together in sensation. If one does vanish in the other, then either the total quantity becomes greater or not. If greater, then the entirety of one line is not fully penetrating the other, contradicting the assumption of complete vanishing. If not greater, then we have two quantities with one vanished into the other but total quantity unchanged. The author then asks why this same reasoning cannot apply to the dimension of the located body and the dimension of space, implying that two dimensions might interpenetrate without increase. This is a strong objection to the Aristotelian position, though they have an excuse to be given later. The passage proceeds step by step, using conditional reasoning and showing contradictions (khalaf) to narrow down the possibilities.

Key Terms
تماس (tamās)
اتصال (ittiṣāl)
طرف (ṭaraf)
خط (khaṭṭ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contact and union are the same thing.
Correction: Contact requires two distinct boundaries, while union involves a single shared boundary. They are mutually exclusive.

Checkpoint
According to the definition, what do the two ends of things in contact have?
A) They are separated by a gap.
B) They are together in position.
C) They merge into one end.
D) They vanish completely.

Key Takeaway
Contact is defined by two distinct boundaries being together; any other case is union.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage draws a sharp line between contact and union, insisting the two must not be confused. In your own understanding of Allah's attributes, is there a place where you have unintentionally merged two distinct concepts (e.g., mercy and justice), losing the precision that protects correct belief? Will you examine one such area this week with the help of a reliable scholar?
?
The author requires that for contact to be real, the two boundaries must remain separate while being together. Where in your life have you blurred the boundary between yourself and others, losing healthy distinction? How might the clarity of this logical principle help you maintain proper spiritual and social boundaries?
?
This lesson challenges you to think carefully about a simple physical scenario and draw a necessary logical conclusion. Are you willing to apply the same rigorous thinking to your faith, even when it feels uncomfortable or inconvenient? What is one belief you hold that you are afraid to examine closely, and will you commit to testing it against revealed truth?

The Dilemma of Remaining Boundaries: Penetration or Separation
Logical sub-divisions of remaining boundaries during contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that if boundaries remain during contact, they must either completely interpenetrate or remain separate, both leading to contradictions.

Core Idea
Remaining boundaries force an impossible choice between separation and penetration.

Having established that boundaries must remain during contact, the author now examines a crucial distinction: do the two lines vanish into one another or not? Each option leads to a logical contradiction. This lesson unpacks these two sub-cases and shows why the Aristotelian definition faces a serious dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Recall that boundaries must remain
From the previous lesson, contact requires that the two boundaries (lines) remain during contact. Now we ask: what happens to these two lines?
2
Explore the first sub-case: one line does not vanish in the other
If the lines do not interpenetrate, they remain separate. Then the sensible indication of each is distinct, contradicting the definition that they are perceived together.
This is a contradiction (khalaf).
3
Explore the second sub-case: one line vanishes completely in the other
If one line completely penetrates the other, then we must check whether the total quantity increases. If it does, the entirety of one line is not fully penetrating the other, again a contradiction.
The author will address the sub-case where quantity does not increase in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أرسطاطاليس، فإنهم قالوا: "المتماسان هما اللذان طرفاهما معاً في الوضع، أي في الإشارة الحسية." قال الشيخ في الشفاء: "والمتماسان هما اللذان تقع الإشارة الحسية على طرفيهما معاً." فأقول: لنفرض سطحاً ماس سطحاً، فههنا يلزم أن يكون طرفاهما معاً، يعني: الخطان اللذان هما نهايتهما يكونان معاً في الوضع. فنقول: هذان الخطان إما أن يكونا باقيين حال حصول هذه المماسة أو لا يكونان باقيين. فإن لم يبقيا بل حدث خط واحد يكون هو بعينه مشتركاً فيه بين ذينك السطحين، فهذا يكون اتصالاً لا مماسة. وقد فرضنا أن الحاصل هو المماسة لا الاتصال. وأيضاً فقول الشيخ: "المتماسان هما اللذان طرفاهما معاً في الوضع"، يقتضي أن يكون طرفاهما باقيين حال التماس، وذلك يمنع من القول بأنهما عدماً وحصل طرف واحد مشترك فيه بين السطحين. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: الطرفان باقيان. فإما أن يقال: نفد أحدهما بالكلية في الآخر أو لم ينفد. فإن لم ينفد أحدهما في الآخر كان أحدهما مبايناً عن الآخر، فيلزم أن لا تكون الإشارة إلى أحدهما عين الإشارة إلى الآخر، مع أنا قد فرضناهما متماسين، هذا خلف. وأما إن قلنا: إن أحد هذين الخطين قد نفد بكليته في كلية الخط الآخر، فإما أن يكون مقدار هذا المجموع أزيد من مقدار الواحد أو لا يكون. فإن كان الأول فحينئذ لا تكون كلية أحد هذين الخطين نافدة في كلية الخط الآخر، وقد فرضنا الأمر كذلك، وهذا خلف. وإن كان الثاني فقد حصل ههنا مقداران ونفد أحدهما في كلية الآخر، مع أنه لم يصر مقدار المجموع أزيد من مقدار الواحد، وإذا عقلنا ذلك في هذه الصورة، فلم لا يجوز مثله في بعد المتمكن وبعد المكان؟ وهذا سؤال قوي على قانون قولهم. ولهم عنه عذر سيأتي ذكره بعد ذلك مع الجواب، إن شاء الله تعالى.

Here it is necessary that their two ends be together, meaning: the two lines that are their boundaries are together in position. So we say: these two lines either remain during the occurrence of this contact or they do not remain. If they do not remain but a single line occurs that is itself shared between those two surfaces, then this would be a union (ittiṣāl), not a contact (tamās). And we have supposed that what occurs is contact not union. Also, the Shaykh's statement: "The two things in contact are those whose two ends are together in position" necessitates that their two ends remain during contact, and that prevents saying that they are annihilated and a single end shared between the two surfaces occurs. For if one of them does not vanish in the other, then one of them is separate from the other, and it follows that the indication of one of them is not identical to the indication of the other, although we have supposed them to be in contact. And if we say that one of these two lines has completely vanished in the entirety of the other line, then either the quantity of this sum is greater than the quantity of the one or not. Checkpoint
If the two boundaries remain but one does not vanish into the other, what is the consequence?
A) They become one line.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a logical puzzle like this, do you tend to rush to a resolution or do you patiently examine every sub-case, even those that seem impossible? This lesson forces you to sit with an unresolved dilemma. Will you commit to embracing intellectual patience in your faith journey, trusting that truth requires thoroughness?
?
This argument shows that easy answers—like 'penetration solves it'—lead to further contradictions. In your own life, where have you accepted a simple answer to a deep theological question without examining all possibilities? Are you ready to revisit that area with the same rigor this lesson demands?
?
The dilemma forces a choice between two unacceptable options. Have you ever felt trapped between two beliefs that both seem true but contradict each other? How does this lesson's method of analyzing all branches before drawing a conclusion challenge you to hold tension in your own heart rather than forcing a premature resolution?
